wholly ceases to operate on the claim.
The law of 1897 did not abolish mining
districts, but it made the office of district
recorder useless by taking away the legal
ity of district records and making the
county seat the only place for a valid rec
ord. The practical effect was to generally
disorganize mining districts. Prospectors
grumbled much at the inconvenience.
Now there will be a general reorganiza
tion of mining districts throughout the
State and location notices will be again
filed with district recorders. The State
law, with its detailed requirements, left
mining districts little latitude for legis
lation, but now they are again the dole
sources of supplementary regulations.
When the law went Into effect district
records were gathered, often with much
trouble, by the County Recorders. In Mr.
Ricketts' opinion such old district re-cords
must remain where they are.
Prospectors will throw up their hats
chiefly at the escape from the assessment
work required within sixty days. Some
such requirement is badly needed when a
man may, locate forty claims and hold
them for nearly two years without doing
a stroke of work on them, and then relo
cate them, if quick enough, and hold
them for another two years.' The effect
of the State law was to vastly lessen. the
number of locations made, as a prospector
could hold only as many claims as he
could put $50 worth of work on in two
months. - Now one may locate as many
claims as lie pleases, and he not only has
until December 31, 1900, to do his $100 worth
of work but any work done this year will
not count on his assessment.- with the
present boom ,In prospecting mineral â¢ re
gions will be staked out in h lively way
from now on. And. according to Mr.
Ricketts, one who located a claim lust be
fore the repeal of the law may drop his
pick and not complete the $30 assessment
work that may be worrying htm. . '
As to tin- use of location notices, printed
according to the State law, they, would be
valid enough, but they are cumbersome
and complicated, and one of the old forms
would be better. . Â
RAPID RISE OF GOLD DREDGING IN
CALIFORNIA. ;
The starting of three hew. gold dredger
calls attention to the fact that dredging:
tor gold is just coming , ( to the front in
California. Wide. interest is being awak
ened in this interesting form of gold min
ing, which surely has a great future in
this State. Within two years some hun
dreds of thousands of dollars have been
invested in this field, which Is rapidly as
suming large importance.
There are now about ten dredgers of all
kinds and sizes on the' Feather, Yuba
American, Trinity, Klamath and Upper
Sacramento rivers.' Two of the largest
and best will be ready for operation with
in a few days. There is no good reason
why there should not be hundreds of gold
dredges working in the State, and there
undoubtedly will be in a few â . ears in
volving investments of millions, giving
employment and trade to many and large
ly increasing the gold output.
There is probably no large gold region
In the world so inviting for this kind of
mining. The, , beds of the lower courses
of the tributaries of the Sacramento. and
of other streams are deep beds of aurifer
ous gravels which can seldom be worked.
Miles of such unworked river beds are as
rich here and there as any ground worked j
In early days. Since the beginning of
gold mining here such beds have been
worked in small areas by wing damming, I
a costly and risky operation which can ;
be conducted but a small part of the
season.- but which is often very profit
able. Bedrock, where the greatest riches
lie. is often buried many feet deep, in
places nearly a hundred feet and in
others but five or ten. One of the notable
mining enterprises of the State is the
turning of the Feather wholly out of its
bed. A successful gold dredge works
such river beds- to bedrock and gets a
considerable percentage of the gold lying
on the bedrock and in its shallow crevices
and can operate all the year. Many de
posits have been worked only to the
water line.
' But the greatest amount of gold-bear
ing gravel is in the benches flanking the j
streams and in old channels, bars and I
basins. There is an immense area of
such ground averaging moderate values
of a few i cents, a cubic yard through
many feet of depth and often rich near j
bedrock. The only economical method |
of working such ground has been the
hydraulic process, and as thousands of
acres of such ground lie where the
hydraulic process cannot be used, - the
gold still waits extraction. A properly
designed gold dredge will work such de
posits -at- a cost- of from 3 to 5 cents a
cubic yard, or as cheaply per yard as a
hydraulic, plant would. work ground. As ;
vast areas' of ground will average 10 to
20 cents or even more; the opportunities
are 'evident. ,â¢-.-â¢
â â¢'.'California, which has led the world in
quartz mining, is late in "getting around j
to this form of the mining industry. It
began in New -Zealand in the early days |
of mining there, has. reached its highest!
development there, and the world turns
to New Zealand to learn the business.
' There has been a steady evolution of .
the gold dredge since then until now it ,
is one of the chief features of the New- |
Zealand mining industry. On the Clutha |
River in the province of Otago on the!
southern island there are seventy-five
dredgers; and there are perhaps a hun- >
d red more elsewhere in New Zealand. j
In quite recent years the success at- :
tamed in New Zealand has attracted at- j
tention throughout the world, and New j
Zealand methods have been the subject
of much inquiry, report and discussion in
Australia; South Africa, Siberia, Canada, I
the United States and elsewhere. Dredges
are now being built or contemplated in |
every auriferous region of the world. ;
Gold dredging in California may be said
to have started about five years ago, when
the Diestlehorst Brothers of Redding
rigged up a primitive contrivance and
went to working the bed of Lower Middle
Creek above Redding. It was a peculiar j
shaped scoop, holding one-third of a cubic
yard: and operated on a pontoon by a j
steam winch at one end, which dragged i
the scoop along the bottom quite like the
first New Zealand plant. But it paid, and
the- Diestlehorsts planned a larger steam j
power scoop dredge of another style- and j
built it on the Klamath. Then they built
another; and the two Diestlehorst dredges
are now operating at Big Oak Bar on the J
Klamath. A similar dredge of compara
tively small size was put to work on the
Upper Sacramento two or three years ago
by others, and then somebody installed on
that stream a dredge of the clam shell
type. No definite information as to cost
cf operation or profits of any of these
dredges has been given, but they work on
and evidently pay.
Three years ago R. H. Postlewalte came
here on" a business trip from New Zea
land, where he had for some years been
building modern dredges on the Clutha,
and seeing the great possibilities of the
business in California he went into it here
and built a dredge on the Yuba River,
which began operating eighteen months
ago. This was the first here of the type
used In New Zealandâ the continuous or
chain bucket* type, in which an endless
chain of buckets is directed against a bed
or hank. The scoop and clam shell dredges
have paid under favorable conditions, the
suction dredge, so efficient for some pur
poses, has always failed in this field, and
the New Zealand type has generally been
adopted,, though the first cost is larger.
When the Postlewalte dredge was In
stalled several large dredging plants had
; been put into successful operation at va
rious places along the Missouri, Inn Mo
ntana, in Colorado and elsewhere through
the western region, where they are now
rapidly multiplying and interesting capi
tal. Â:-,â¢.;-- 5^.,.; y : :.-, :
The success of the Postlewalte dredge
on the Yuba has been followed by the
building of five more, making six great
dredges of the modern type. One is on
the Trinity, one on the American, one on
tin- Yuba and three are on the Feather
low Oroville a few miles. These dredges
are all similar. There are two pontoons
about 100 feet long connected so as to
practieallv form one boat of that length.
23 feet wide, with a well hole five feet
wide and 75 feet long, through which is
operated the ladder or girder, up and
down which the chain of buckets travels.
This girder will, with its buckets, reach
ground 45 feet below water or quite a dis
tance above. Thirty-eight heavy, steel
lipped buckets or scoops travel at the rate
of fifteen per minute, driven by a 45-horse
power engine. These SCOOps will pick up
boulders of a ton weight, will dig into
hard ground and will scrape and dig up
the top of the bedrock. At the top the
buckets empty their material, which goes
into a big revolving screen or grizzly sup
plied with 3000 gallons of water per min
ute. The fine material is washed through
the screen and passes to plates and to
sluices fitted with cocoa matting and rif
fles, where 98 per cent of the gold is said
to be saved. The tailings and the boulders
from the grizzly go to an endless carrier,
which reaches out behind and which will
stack the debris 26 feet high. The ma
chinery is massive to stand the shocks
and wear. One of these dredges is quite
a complicated and striking affair. Each
has a dynamo driven by a separate en
gine for electric lights, and a five-barrel
steam winch for the cables which radiate
from the- corners and straight ahead to
hold the dredge in place and to move it.
Such a dredge costs $35,000. and In favor
able ground will handle 2500 cubic yards
per twenty-four hours. In Montana and
Colorado such dredging plants have in
cases been put on wheels running on a
track, but wherever water can be had
floating is preferred. These dredges are
operated twenty-four hours a day by
three shifts of only two men each. The
only other necessary employes are one
dredge master and a general superintend
ent.
Some of these dredges have been
"launched" like any boat, but others have
not. The one on the Trinity was built
nine months ago, and is owned by
Messrs. Oilman and McNeill of Chicago
who bought nearly four I miles of placer
ground about Lowden's" ranch, ten miles
above "Wcavervllle. The 1 dredger was put
up in an excavation nearly a mile from
the river, on an old bar, and a "sluice was
started un stream 'to supply water to
float . and- -operate the dredge. The
dredge floated off and went to work at the
gravel about It. There Is a great area
of pay gravel twenty-five to thirty feet
deep, enough to keep several â¢ dredgers
busy many years.

i inches beam and' displaces 15,200' tons on

two sets of engines have a collective

Inch, fourteen 6-inch quick firers, eight
; 3 and four 2 1 . pounders. The maximum
thickness of the armor belt is nine inches,
i and the gun deck is protected by 6-inch
I armor. The coal bunker capacity is 1100
: tons.

WHEAT ON THE
SACRAMENTO
3000 Sacks Losi
Overboard.
TRANSPORTS GETTING READY
BRUTUS SAILS TO-DAY AND
THREE OTHERS FOLLOW.
Changes on the Nippon Maru.
Wharfinger Tom Casey on a Fish
ing Excursion Visitors
to the lowa.
Through some one's blundering three
thousand sacks of grain were lost in the
Sacramento River last Saturday night.
The steamer Ban Jose was on her way
here with a barge in tow. On the barge
were four thousand sacks of wheat for
the British ship Primrose Hill. Just how-
It happened no one seems to know, but the
barge tilted over during the night and
nearly all the cargo went Into the river.
Some of it was recovered and put aboard
the San Jose and that vessel came on to
San Francisco, leaving the barge half un
der water and tied up to the bank.
During the run down the river the
wheat on the San Jose began to swell and
the bags burst. The grain ran all over
the decks, and when the steamer reached
the seawall it had to l- shoveled ashore.
The portion saved is now only fit for
chicken feed, so the entire barge load of
4000 sacks is practically a total loss.
There ara now forty-three foreign and
Atlantic vessels ln port, and of these one
8 loading wheat and four only are bound
for Europe. The Halewood came here
from Pisagua with a load of nitre, and she
Is going in ballast to ipilla to load
the same kind of a cargo for England.
The Primrose Hill will take wheat for
"stiffening" and general merchandise to
Liverpool: the Routenburn is at Eppin
ger's loading wheat for England, and the
Strathdon. as soon as she- discharges her
load of coal into the United States trans
port Arizona, will load redwood for Cork.
Of the others the Bldston Hill is under
charter to take 9. load of supplies for the
United States Government to Manila, the
Cardigan Castle takes supplies to Samoa
for the United States coaling station at
Pago Pago, the M. P. Grace goes to Port
Blakelev to load lumber for South Africa
and the' Prussia and Sonoma go into the
coasting coal trade. The disengaged ton
nage In port on Saturday was 31,006, while
at the same time last year it was only
psfirt. The engaged tonnage amounted to
24.373 tons, while last year it was 39,277.
Of the engaged fleet seven vessels are on
the berth for Hawaiian ports, two for
Siberian ports and two for Mexico. The
ship Columbia and the schooner Nokomls
are also loading for Siberia, but as they
belong to the coasting fleet they do not
appear in the above figures.
Tom Casey, the popular wharfinger at
Steuart street wharf, went on a fishing
expedition yesterday. In his young days
"Tom was a noted fisherman, and many
a yarn he spins of the "fish that got
away." in the chief wharfinger's office.
He was determined to make a good show
ing on this occasion in order to keep up
his reputation, so he took a can of giant
powder along.
The United States collier Brutus finished
taking aboard her cargo yesterday and
will sail for Guam at 10 a. m. to-day. The
transport Scandla goes on the Hunters
Point drydock to-day. and to-morrow she
will go alongside Folsom street wharf,
â¢where the Alaska Iters' Association
has made room for her. The transport
Arizona finished coaling from the British
ship Strathdon yesterday and will begin
receiving supplies to-day. The Newport
will dock at the Union Iron Works to
day md to-morrow she will begin loading
at" the Mail Company's wharf. The
troops for Manila are now on their way
here, and as soon as they arrive the
transports will be ready to receive them.
Before next Monday the chances are that
all three vessels will l.Â» on their way.
The Toyo Risen Kaisha's steamer Nip
pon Maru will sail for the Orient next
Wednesday, loaded down to the guards
with freight. Every berth has been taken,
r.nd in order to get a passage many peo
ple will sleep on cots. Purser W. H. Ma
gee will not go out on the vessel, his place
being taken by H. Priver, one of the O.
and O. employes. The freight clerk also
remains asno,re. and Dr. Deas of the Har-
Ibor Receiving Hospital will go out in Dr.
Fine's place. The change of doctors will
be only temporary; that of purser and
freight clerk will be permanent.
The schooner Pearl sailed for Unalaska
yesterday. She Ib the first of the Alaska
Commercial Company's fleet to get away,
and will be followed by the steamer
Bertha In about a month.
The launch William D carried passen
gers to the battle-ship lowa yesterday.
The Tow-as engines are being overhauled,
and it will be some weeks yet before she
comes down from the Union Iron Works.
The launch Amy is being rebuilt and will
be in commission again next Sunday,
when she and the William D will carry
visitors to the battle-ship.
SUICIDE OF HENRY ROTHSCHILD
Despondency Brought About by a
Falling Off in His Saloon
Business.
Henry RothschHd, who had conducted a
saloon at the northwest corner of O'Far
rell and Powell streets for a number of
years, died at his home, 815 Polk streef,
yesterday morning at 4:30 o'clock from
strychnine poisoning. The only reason
assignable for Rothschild's death Is that
he became despondent over his business,
which had been falling away during the
past year. Previous to his going Into the
saloon business he conducted a grocery
store it the place for a number of years,
but the section having lost its family
character for that of a choice tenderloin,
â wide open, sporty nature he sought bar
room business exclusively and failed. The
adverse change preyed on his mind and
caused him to take his life 1% the manner
described. ' ... .-;
Friday night Rothschild closed up his sa
loon as usual about 3 o'clock and went to
his home. The same morning when called
for breakfast by his wife he complained
of feeling sick ahd remained at the house
during the day. He was attended by Dr.
Frederick T. Bond, but nothing could be
done to save his life. His -widow and
daughter had attributed his death to an
accident caused by his drinking whisky
out of a bottle that had contained rough
on rats and which had not been cleaned
since the deadly mixture had been in It.
Coroner Hill, however. ignores the acci
dental cause and is pronounced In his
opinion that Rothschild commit!- suicide
with deliberate intent. Out of a feeling
of kindness for the family Dr. Hill per
mitted the body to remain at the resi
dence, whÂ«Â»re an autopsy will be held to
day. The deceased leaves a widow and
four young children to mourn over his sad
death.

Found in the Bay.
The body of an unknown man was
found floating in the bay below long
bridge yesterday afternoon. Deceased
was about 60 years of age, was dressed
like a laborer and had apparently been
in the water about two days. He had evi
dently been an inmate of the City and
County Hospital, as he wore a night shirt
such as is used in that institution. There
was a fresh wound on his right side as
though an abscess had recently been re
moved. The body is lying at the Morgue
awaiting identification.

HE BUILT A HORSELESS
CARRIAGE OF HIS OWN
A MONTH ago there Issued
from the basement of the
residence of J. A. Meyer, 1415
Twenty-fourth street, a
horseless carriage that Is
now the pride of Noe Val
ley. Ever since, on pleasant days, it
.__ been careering over the streets
if the city, distancing the speediest
roadsters and increasing the colony
of rubbernecks, for which this city is
justly famous. They cannot resist
the temptation to turn their heads
when Meyer's machine whizzes by.
The machine is entirely the product
of the brain and hands of Mr. Meyer.
He is foreman for the J. P. Hicks Gas
Engine Company, a designer and me
chanic of ability. Twelve years ago
while in the employ of Baker & Ham
ilton at Benicia, long before any one
else thought of such a thing, he was
seized with a desire to make ,a horse
less carriage. He went as far as con
structing the engine, when unexpected
difficulties arose, and he abandoned
the project, storing the engine In his
basement on Twenty-fourth street,
where it still stands. A sterling trib
ute was given to his skill in 1593. At
that time he exhibited at the Me
chanics' Fair a miniature Corliss en
gine, which "'was run during the expo
sition and for which he was given a
diploma of honor. ' ', '.-_-. ?
Meyer never abandoned the idea of
the horseless carriage, but did no
work upon It until two years ago,
when he happened to see running
through the park the vehicle' Imported
by Charles Fair. At that the ancient
fever seized him and he set to work,
the result being the perfect machine
that now carries himself, his wife and
children at a faster pace and in finer
style than any nabob In the city. At
the present time the machinery is per
MUSICAL SERVICES
AT EMMANUEL BAPTIST
HANDSOME NEW PIPE ORGAN
IS DEDICATED.
Rev. J. George Gibson, the Pastor,
Delivers an Eloquent Sermon
Before a Large Congre
gation.
The Emmanuel Baptist Church was
crowded last night by a congregation as
sembled to attend the dedication of the
handsome new pipe organ. The altar and
the , aisle approaches were handsomely
decorated with flowers. The new organ Is
built on the left of the chancel and well
situated with a view to the attainment' of
the best acoustic effects.
The services were musical in character.
The barytone solo, "Glory to Christ.. Our
King." was sung with deep emotion and
full solemnity. "The Lord of the Ages"
â¢was rendered by Mr. Russel Dowry with
great feeling. The last solo was a hymn
by Miss A. Lahl.
Rev. J. George Gibson, the pastor, de
livered the evening sermon, selecting for
his text "The Song of Moses and the
Lamb." He spoke of man's mission in life
and the fruits which come from a life of
Integrity, honor and truth.
The new organ has been raised by sub
scription and represents in a great meas
ure the unceasing labors of the pastor.

fect, working without -a hitch, but it
is not covered by the stylish box, a
four-seated phaeton affair, that
Meyer is . having built for It. As a
temporary affair he has a wagon
body made of redwood, which answers
all the purposes of exhibition.
As it stands, Meyer's horseless car
riage weighs SOO pounds, and at its
highest speed can be whisked over the
ground at the rate of eighteen miles
an hour. The motive power is fur
nished by a three and a half horse
power engine, weighing 200 pounds,
which is carried under the rear seat,
this is fed with naphtha gas, which It
aerates itself, and which is Ignited
In the explosion chamber 'if the cylin
der by a spark produced by an eight-
.-11 dry battery. The exhaust is taken
up so perfectly in condensers under
the wagon body that there Is neither
noise nor smoke. The wheels have
solid cushion tires and roller bear
ings, assuring an easy, noiseless mo
tion, as was proved by a ride on the
carriage over none too smooth a
road. The combination brake and
steering gear and the water circula
tion in the engine are absolutely per
fect.
The naphtha tank will contain
enough of that fluid to generate gas
for a run of twelve hours, and enough
more can be carried in cans to triple
the running radius. Altogether, it
seems as perfect a machine as could
be turned out of any factory.

DEVELOPMENT IN SHASTA.
Lumber Mill and a Railroad to Be
Built in That County.
Cogglns Bros., the well-known lumber
men of Shasta County, are negotiating for
another large tract of timber land in that
county with the Southern Pacific Com
pany. They possess several large pieces
In the vicinity of Slate Creek, on the west
side of the Sacramento River, north of
Delta. They propose to build a railroad
from their lands to Slatonls, on the Cali
fornia and Oregon road, about two and a
half miles north of Delta. The length
of the road will be about twelve miles.
They will also put up large mills for the
purpose- of working up the lumber that Is
cut. Work on this enterprise is to be
commenced shortly.
Took Carbolic Acid.
'Mrs. Alice Cochrane, 30 years of age,
was found in her room at 734 Montgomery
street shortly after 6 o'clock last night
suffering from carbolic acid poisoning.
She was taken to the Harbor Receiving
Hospital, where it was ascertained that
she had not taken enough of the drug to
cause death. Her husband, who Is a
longshoreman, left her some time ago and
since then she has been keeping a lodging
house. The venture failed, and a few
days ago she closed the house.

CABLE CAR AND
BADLY INJURED
Mrs. Beach's Serious
Accident.
CARELESS GRIPMAN AGAIN
STARTED THE . CAR WHILE SHE
WAS ALIGHTING.
Refused to Stop After the Accident
I and Her -Year-Old Son
Jumped, Also Hurting
j. ' Himself.
Criminal, carelessness on th,e part of j
employes of the Market Street Railway \
I Company may' result fatally in the case ;
; of Mrs. James Beach, wife of Policeman \
', James Beach,' who' was thrown from a '
i street car last Saturday afternoon. The j
! lady resides at 3547 Twenty-second street, !
and was about to transfer to a Fillmore j
' street car from Haight street, on the re
turn from the park to her home, when she j
I met with an accident serious in the ex-
I treme. .'-'--.â â  "">: : .^-
'Mrs: Beach , was on board Haight street !
car No. 195, and had secured her transfer j
to "Fillmore street. She attempted to i
alight from the car, but the grlpman, re
gardless of her effort, started the vehicle.
She was thrown violently to the ground
and ' badly injured about the head and I
chest. The woman was in a delicate con
dition, and grave results are feared as a i
The car continued on its journey, and
Mrs.' Beach's little four-year-old son, who
accompanied -her, and who still remained
on the car, called to both conductor and
grlpman to stop. Neither of them paid
any attention to the child, and he
jumped to the ground about fifty feet
from where his mother was lying. He
also received several cuts and bruises.
Last evening Mrs. Beach was In a crit
ical : condition, and the attending physi
cians entertain but little hope of her re
covery. The family are properly in
censed over the affair, and a suit will
shortly be commenced against the rail
way company.-

Harvey Smith Thrown From a Car
at Fourth ana Harrison.
Harvey Smith, an old man residing at
16 Shotwell street, Was thrown from a
Fourth street car at Fourth and Harri
son streets early this morning by Motor
man'William Brown, sustaining a severe
scalp Wound. The man was a little under
the Influence of liquor and, the motorman
says, was taken with an epileptic tit.
which required his ejection. Two wit
nesses, Charles L. Kinard of 60S 1 -- Fran
cisco street and A. Hogrede of I_4^_ Sil
ver street,- say that Brown used much
force in ridding himself or the objection
able passenger. When Smith fell his head
struck the cobblestones and it was at
first thought that his skull was fractured,
but at the Receiving Hospital It was
found that his wound was not of so seri
ous a nature. : :''~,:S< -

"The Power of Example."
Yesterday being Low Sunday, Rev. E.
B. Spalding, pastor of St. John's Epis
copal ' Church, preached from the text,
"So, even i here unto, were ye called, be
cause Christ also suffered for us. leaving
us. an example that' ye should follow, in
this step," First Peter xi:2l. The power of
human example was touched upon, and
more especially the example which Christ
has set us in all things from the least to
the greatest and which Christian people
are bound to follow. Five essential at
tributes were 'named as noticeable in
those who i follow. in his stepsâ humility,
suffering, self-sacrifice, work and love.
At the evening service Dr. Spaulding also
spoke upon r the- power of example. â¢
Cut ,TV \i h a Beer Glass.
Thomas Maguire, an ex-baseball player
and now employed; in the' navy yard at
i Mare-Island, went : into the saloon at 35
! Eddy street yesterday afternoon and
I quarreled with August M. Meyer, the pro- j
j prietor, about drinks. He struck at Meyer,
! but could not reach' him, so he picked up
a beer glass and threw it at him. The
I glass hit Meyer: on the left ear and neck,
cutting both. Meyer, was taken to the
Receiving . Hospital, and Maguire to the
City Prison by Policeman W. ,W. Wilson,
where a charge of assault with a deadly
weapon was booked against him.

IMPRESSIVE CEREMONIES
GENTLEMEN'S ; SODALITY AT ST.
IGNATIUS. " " ~ ;;- ;^
Father MacCorry Says Mass on l>oard
"the â Father Wyman's Ser
mon on the "Resurrec- '
tion." '' -
St. Joseph's Church, corner of Tenth
and Howard streets,' was crowded at the
S o'clock mass yesterday, it being the
date for the annual receiving of holy
communion by ,the Ancient Order, of Hi
bernians. The , members "of the well
known organization met in their head
quarters at 120 Ninth street at 7:15 a. m.,
and, led by their officers,' nearly 400
marched to the church and occupied seats
In the body of the edifice. Father Crow
ley of the Youths' Directory said mass
and .administered communion to the.so
ciety. He also delivered a short address
complimenting the men on their splendid
organization, and . appealed to | them -to
continue their good work. Music appro
priate to the occasion was rendered by
the St. Joseph's choir.. - â¢ -
Seven hundred members of the Gentle
men's Sodality attended the 7:30 mass at
St. Ignatius Cathedral and fulfilled their
Paschal duties. The members wete at
tired in their, regalia. Father Hlckey, di
rector of the sodality, was celebrant. Im
mediately after mass the, men marched
to their headquarters, and concluded the
ceremonies by reciting the Tedeum.
At. St. Mary's Cathedral the boys and
girls of the cathedral parish received
their first communion. Vicar General the
Very Rev. J. J. Prendergast -celebrated
-mass. As the children entered the sa
cred edifice the organist played Mozart's
"Gloria." After they had sung a hymn
Father Prendergast said mass, accompan
ied by the organ. At the conclusion of
the service Father, Prendergast-Instruct
ed the young ones. At 11 o'clock mass
Father Prendergast- delivered; the sermon
and mass was sung by Father Dempsey.
In the afternoon. the children .assembled
in the cathedral, enrolled In the scapular
and given further instruction by Father
J. B. Hanlgan. Â« > - , -, â¢
At the special Invitation of Chaplain
Brown of the battleship lowa, Father
MacCorry, C. S. P., celebrated mass on
ship board yesterday morning. The serv
ices were- Impressive and were attended
by, nearly the entire crew. The eloquent
young priest delivered a short but effec
tive discourse during the services. ,- ."â¢?.
Father Wvman delivered a brilliant ser
mon on "The Power tof the Resurrection"
at St. Mary's. In part, he said:
"There is no doctrine preached by the
Apostles that excited greater opposition
among their hearers than the doctrine of
the Resurrection. The Pharisees opposed
It because of their hatred of Christ and;
their unwillingness to believe that "one
whom they had put to death' as. a male
factor was the 'first fruits of them that
Bleep.' The Sadducees rejected it as an
Impossibility. "As Holy Scripture says,
they believed neither in Resurrection { nor
spirit nor angel. - r "
"When also St. Paul preached the doc
trine at Athens, some of the Greeks
mocked; others said, 'We will hear you
again about this matter.' 'There were
even doubts and difficulties among pro
fessing Christians on this great . point,
which had to be cleared up by; St. Paul.
Perhaps no fact indicates more -clearly
the almost universal disbelief in this doc
trine, among the civilized Romans . than
that cremation was a common method- of
disposing of the dead.. . Â_,'â¢'â¢
"The Christian doctrine of the - Resur
rection is essential to the belief in mans
immortality, because man is a composite
being, having both, soul and body. The
union of the soul and body In man is sub
stantial, hence if man Is. to live hereafter
in a perfect state his body after death
must be united to his soul." â¢'.;:;;
AT PARK AND BEACH.
Owing to the Cold Weather Crowds
Were Small.
It was cold and disagreeable at the park
and . beach yesterday, and hte small
crowds that ventured from their
homes to seek - pleasure- '. in the
open soon . returned to shelter. The
drive was small, as were, the number of
wheelmen. No accidents occurred to liven
matters up. so altogether it was a 'very
dull day.
Notwithstanding the ' cold ' weather an
average crowd visited the Chutes. A new
illusion and the London door maze- have
been added to the many attractions^ at
the grounds; and - were well - patronized
throughout the day. Many. people also

OAKLAND TRIES
TO PLAY WITH A
CRIPPLED TEAM
Two Men Sent to the
Repair Shop.
A CHAPTER OF ACCIDENTS
HAMMOND AND DUNLEAVY ARE
BADLY INJURED.
Hardie Breaks All the Catchers'
Masks, Murphy Loses His Shoes
and San Jose Has an
Easy Time.
After sending two of his men off to the
hospital in an ambulance and digging
around among the bleachers for volun
teers to fill up the gap in his ranks. Man
ager Ewing yesterday watched his Oak
land misfits give way before the prune
pickers in .1 game replete with swats and
mispiays.
The slaughter occurred right at the out
set of the game, when his two catchers
were carried off the battlefield In rapid
succession. This so -heartened the
others, particularly Moskiman, that they
were unable to make any stand against
the giants from the prune belt. Moski
man. who is usually a mystery to oppos
ing batsmen, could do nothing better than
sail straight, juicy ones up the main
ais'.e, fifteen of which were dispatched to
portions of the field that were unpro
tected.
San Jose was first at bat and Hammond
donned the mask and air cushion for the â 
cripples. Hardly a half dozen balls had
been pitched before Hammond was caught
with a foul tip between the thumb and
forefinger of his right hand, splitting that
member down to the center of the palm.
He was sent to the Receiving Hospital
and Dunleavy called in from center field,
Shea, the change pitcher, who was play
ing on the bench, going into the field. San
Jose was retired without a run and Oak
land went to bat.
Dunleavy led off with a beautiful three
cushion shot, but had to slide into the bag
to beat the ball. He went # in feet first
and caught his foot in the strap, giving
his ankle a terrible wrench. He was as
sisted to the clubhouse and into his street
clothes, and as soon as his shoe was re
moved his ankle swelled to the size of his
calf. -
Game had to be called until Manager
Ewing could find somebody to fill out the
team. Finally Ford, who caught last
season for the Athletics, was drafted,
and while he was getting into a uniform,
"Clam Juice Lou" endeavored to adjust
himself to a mask, as it was the Intention
to have him go behind the bat. Hardies
head became so enlarged at the thought
of getting back Into his old-time posi
tion that he broke the straps to both of
the face-protectors, and there being no
birdcages In the neighborhood for him
to use- as a substitute, he was. perforce,
compelled to continue In the right
meadow, while Ford did the receiving.
This closed Oakland's chapter of ac
cidents with the exception of Murphy,
who forgot his Working shoes and was
obliged to play in his street footgear,
which destroyed his lack of confidence In
himself when he attempted to move
about. But the team had lost heart, and
that meant the game. Lange made two
costly errors, one of which, combined
with a bungle by Lee Hammond, re
sulted in six runs in the- fourth inning.
Klelber made some embalmed-beef
plays for San Jose, but Fatty will always
make- errors, for he never shirks any
thing that comes anywhere near him.
The triple that Dunleavy tore off in the
first netted a run for Oakland, which was
balanced In the second by San Jose.
Eagan getting a single and completing
the circuit on Mosklman's throw Into the
field to head off Andrews at first. They
got another in the third, two singled and
a long fly to Hardie letting Foley get
around.
The fourth Inning tore the life out of
the game. Klelber. the first man up, was
safe on Hammond's fumble. Eagan was
given a pass and Andrews hit to Mos
kiman. who threw to Lange to head off
Klelber. The ball struck Fred's hands
fairly and bounded out, Kleiber scoring
and Andrews reaching first, when a
double play was in order, which would
have retired the side. Andrews tried to
steal second, but was thrown out by
Ford, who duplicated the performance
twice afterward, to the intense disgust
of the blond twirler. Then came the
bombardment on Moskiman. Kent, Mc-
Gucken, Foley, Anderson and Hanlon
followed with singles, which with Dal
rymple'B long fly to Hardie scored five
more tallies, a total of six for the inning.
San Jose finished Its run-getting in the
fifth, scoring one run, although the first
four men up made nice, safe shots. Eagan
led off with a single, stole second and
scored on Andre drive. The pitcher
made another futile attempt to steal sec
ond, when if he had waited he could have
gone the rounds on Kent's and MeGucken's
safe shots. As it was, both of those men
were left on bases.
Oakland tallied again in that inning.
Murphy getting around on errors by
Klelber and Andrews and a single by
Hammond. Hits by Murphy. Lange and
Hammond gave them one more run in the
ninth, but It was of no importance as far
as the outcome was effected.
Ford has been signed to do the catching
for Oakland until W. Hammond gets back
into the game, and he may be retained
permanently, for Borland, who was signed
because of his batting powers, is not hit
ting at all this season. Manager Ewing
will also replace Murphy at first as soon
as lie can find a good baseman. Murphy
is a fine player and hard hitter, but he
Ib unable to get away from his business
to play during the week, and the team is
badly handicapped thereby.
The team is in a bad way just now, for
Donovan, who was compelled to retire
from Saturday's game, will - not be in
shape again for two or three weeks, and
with W. Hammond and Dunleavy laid up
it takes three of the best run-getters out
of the game. The score:

Uncle Wins the Series.
WATSONVILLE. April 9.-In the pres
ence of a large and enthusiastic audience
of baseball fans the Watsonville "Sugar
Beets" succumbed to Uncle Hank Harris'
Frisco lads to-day by a score of 3 to 1.
I: was one of the most interesting games
ever played here. The only two errors
were made by Catcher Morrow of the
Watsonvilles. Both were on throws to
second and were responsible for one run.
Both Iberg and Whalen were batted free
ly, but their excellent support saved them
from more base hits. Trie Watsonville
lads were very unsuccessful in landing
their hits in safe territory. The home
team redeemed themselves for the poor
playing done yesterday, which was due,
no doubt, to lack of harmony among the
players. The public was again disap
pointed to-day !n not seeing -a regular
league umpire present. Below is the score:

Senators Defeat the Lobsters.
SACRAMENTO, April 9.â Fifteen hun
dred people saw the Statesmen defeat the
Santa Cruzans this afternoon by a score
of 6 to 4. The game was very fast from
the start, and interest never flagged. The
locals took three runs in the first and
three in the second inning, while the
Beachcombers crossed the plate three
times in the fourth and once in the sixth.
The feature of the game was Clark's long
drive over right field fence, a thing which
never before had been accomplished on
the Oak Park diamond. -,'-â¢;: *â¢â)-

Woodland Bicycle Xl.c?s.
WOODLAND, April 9.â A fair crowd at
tended the bicycle tournament. As a
strong wind blew and the track was soft
no records were broken. The one-mile
tandem handicap, professional, was won
by Cotter and Barnaby. An exciting ten
mile relay race between teams of ten men
each of the Capital City Wheelmen and
followed. The" Woodland "team won by
less than a foot. The -mile professional
dash was taken by John Lawson. Frank
Cotter of Tacoma won the five-mile handi
cap, with, an advantage of sixty yards.
Ivor Lawson stopped at the end. of the
fourth mile.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, APRIL 10, 1899.
SHORTENDERS
BROUGHT HOME
GOOD MONEY
Gladiator Won at
Union Park.
r/t.r. â 
RUSTY GOLD RUNNER UP
METALLIC WAS THE GOOD THING
â¢ OF THE DAY.
Fleet Maid of the Hill Captured
the Extra Stake in
Very Easy Fash
ion.
* Gladiator added another stake to his
credit yesterday at the Union Coursing
Park, winning the open stake in masterly
fashion. The dog is 'the property of
James Dean, and in all his courses he
showed both the speed and condition over
his company. The runner-up was Royal
Flush, who ran in remarkably fine form.
Emm Pasha was the surprise of the day.
making a grand showing, and was not put
out until the sixth round by the runner
up. 'i>.vC-;-\: c ;'
Never before In the history of the park
have the shortenders had such a success
ful day. In the third round of the open
stake six dogs on the short end secured
the winning flag in quick succession. The
first was Metallic, who defeated that good
hound Connnemara by a very flunky
course. The lead-up. was made by Conne
mara, but the hare swung round to Me
tallic and he made a drive and kill. Jen
nie Wilson was next on the list, defeating
Firm Friend. Emm Pasha was right in
line with the procession, being slipped to a
hare with False Flatterer. The black and
white dog ran like a demon, and after a
few turns killed, with the Flatterer point
less. Emm Pasha's price was the longest
of the day, pools being sold on him as
good as 4 to 1. Glen Roy and Koolawn
were the other good things in this round.
i Ida ran abominably, and is either a
"lurcher" or in bad condition. She ran
with Shylock Boy and was unable to get
up the least resemblance of a run.
The "extra" stake was won by the Maid
of the Hill. A few of the wise ones touted
Black Night as a good thing, but the best
she could do was to get second money.
Mission Tip was the only shortender that
won in the stake, defeating Winning
Ways at 2% to 1.

Coursing at Stockton.
STOCKTON, April Dog days have
come sure enough. With two coursing
clubs in full blast, good hounds and a
lively Interest, Stockton seems destined
to become a point of interest for leash
men. The San Joaquin Club has a rival
now in the Stockton Coursing Club, and
the promoters of each are playing for
public favor by putting up the best
cards possible Both will increase their
prize money in order to secure the best
hounds and the favor of the talent. The
first and second prizes to-day were $40
and $25. with a lot of coin thrown arouno
for consolation.

Los Angeles Coursing.
LOS ANGELES. April 9.â The somewhat
chilly weather to-day had the effect of
diminishing the attendance at the
coursing at Agricultural Park, espe
cially in the grand stand, though there
were at least 2500 people on the grounds
and the sport was good.
The programme comprised two stakes â
a 16-dog sapling and a 32-dog openstake.
The sapling stake was the first ever run
in Southern California, and another sap
ling of twenty-four dogs Is announced for
next Sunday to accommodate the first
crop of puppies that have come into the
running since coursing was -inaugurated
here.' For the saplings First Call took
first money,' with Ida May second and
Me's Rosebud third.
In the open stake Lass O'Gowne took
first money, with Master Jack second and
Mountain Belle third. There were several
surprises, the most noticeable of which
was when Lass O'Gowne put out the
crackajack Merry Maiden in the first ties.
The dogs as a rule ran true to form,
though all bets were declared off in the
decision.
WANT TO RESUME.
Ingleside Coursing Club Would Like
to Open When the Races Close.
The Ingleside Coursing Club, which sus
pended operations at its grounds at In
gleside as soon as the ordinance passed
by the Supervisors stopping betting both
on horse races and coursing, expects to
resume operations at its grounds as soon
as the racing season is over in May. The
offici of the club seriously contemplat
ed building a coursing park outside the
county line, but no suitable site could be
selected. The most available quarters
that the club members wanted was a
strip of land near the lakes of the Spring
Valley Water Company, beyond the coun
ty line, but after a great amount of work
and time had been spent in making pre
liminary steps to rent the land they dis
covered they could not lease the prop
erty for any length of time. What steps
the Police Department will take in the
matter when the club resumes operations
remains to be proved, but the general
belief among the sporting fraternity 01
the city is thai the club is bluffing, and
that betting on coursing will not be
countenanced IU the city, whether horse
racing is running or not.
CAPTAIN GRIEB
PROCLAIMED KING
The Red Men Schuetzen Company held
its annual picnic and eagle slloot at Shell
Mound Park yesterday. Forty members
of the company assembled in uniform at
San Francisco Turners' Hall on Turk
street and proceeded to the ferry under
command of the following officers: Cap
tain H. Grieb, First Lieutenant P. H. Will
and Second Lieutenant Conrad Stein.
The committee of arrangements consist-
Ed of President C. G. Boisdorff, Secretary
M. Fuetscher, Treasurer Conrad Stein,
George Heuer, D. Walpern, H. Schlcher
and William Mahrmann.
Arriving at the park the picnickers to
the number of over a thousand began to
enjoy themselves, dancing in the pavilion
to the music of an excellent band being
the main amusement.
The marksmen of the company contest
ed in an eagle shoot for the King medal
and $50 in coin, constituting the first prize,
and eleven other valuable prizes. The
prizes were won In the following order:
Captain Grleb (king). D. Walpern, J. D.
Feldermann, Oscar Heuer, H. Rulffs, M.
Dlechert, B. Fenschel, J. A. Mohr, H.
Grleb and C. Oldag.
The distribution of prizes will take place
on Wednesday evening at Turn Verein
Hall, Turk street.
DELIBERATELY INSULT
MEXICO'S EMBASSADOR
WASHINGTON, April 9.â lt is painfully
apparent European Embassadors here
have determined to boycott their new
colleague, Mr. Aspiroz of Mexico. There
have been suspicious circumstances from
the hour of his arrival, but the officials of
our Government and Washington society
generally have been reluctant to believe
such eminent and broad minded states
men as Sir Julian Paunceforte, Count Cas
sill, Dr. yon Holleben, Jules Cambon and
others would deliberately humiliate a
stranger, and one so amiable and honor
able as the Embassador from Mexico.
They no longer conceal their inten
tions, and Count; Lichdervelde, the Bel
gian Minister, who telegraphed to Brus
sels for instructions as to the course he
should pursue, has been directed by his
Government to stand by the representa
tives of European powers and freeze the
Mexican out. -
They did not call upon him before his
presentation. When he called . upon
them they were met at home, and none
of them appeared at his reception.
This might have been due to a series of
accidents, but it was no accident that
every member of the diplomatic corps
sent regrets to the reception which ex-
Secretary and Mrs. John W. Foyer gave
in honor of Mr. and Mrs. Aspiroz last
Wednesday evening, which was not only a
slight to the guests of that evening but
to the hosts also.
Yesterday afternoon at a tea given by
Countess Lichderveld, wife of the Bel
gian Minister, the truth came out. The
Mexican Embassador and Mrs. Aspiroz
were the only members of the diplomatic
corps who were not present, and their
absence was due to a failure to reveice
invitations. It was a deliberate insult.
The pretext for the boycott lies in the
historical fact' that thirty years ago Mr
Aspiroz happened to be the Judge Advo
cate. General of the Mexican Army, who
was detained by President Juarez" to try-
Pretender Maximilian, the Austrian arch
duke, who, under protection of Louis
Napoleon of France, claimed the throne
of Mexico. But why the performance of
a military duty under orders from his
Government by- Mr. Aspiroz a third of a
century ago should make _lm an unlit
associate of the British, German. French
Russian and Italian embassadors is diffi
cult to understand.
Great Britain and Germany refused to
sustain Maximilian and practically re I
pudiated the Invasion of Mexico and it
is not believed either of these govern
ments-will approve the action of its Em
bassador in declaring a boycott against
his colleague upon such; a pretext if they
are appealed to.
ASSAULTED HER
LOVER WITH AN
EGG SANDWICH
Opening of the Re
sort at El Campo.
. â 
PICNICKERS HAVE GOOD TIME
SEVERAL SCRAPS UNATTENDED
BY BLOOD.
The Singing of a Ribald Song Nearly
Results in the Surprised Au
thor Being Thrown Over
board.
Amid scenes of revelry and mirth, occa
sionally interrupted by a fight, which re
sulted in no serious damage to the con
testants, the picnic resort at El Campo
was thrown open to the public yester
day. Several hundred people attended the
opening, evidently bent on having a good
time.
The superintendent of the grounds, an
ticipating a large crowd of picnickers,
made every arrangement for their com
fort. A band of fourteen pieces was in at
tendance, and at the conclusion of the
day's festivities the wind jammers were
in a condition bordering on apoplexy.
Immediately after the arrival of the 2
o'clock boat, which carried a large crowd
to the picnic grounds, the fun began.
A young man who was known to his as
sociates as "Teddy," evidently jealous of
a south of Market dude who was dancing
with his best girl, swore to be revenged.
Summoning another youth, who was
staggering under a load of bad beer,
"Teddy" confided to him his intention of
sending the dude to the Morgue.
"It's this way," angrily declared the
jealous suitor, -'me and Nell have been
friends for years, and I'm dead stuck on
her. See! Now, this mug shows up and
cops her out. 1 s'pose I'm a dead one
wid her. but I want ter get even; are
you on?"
"You want me to soak him, eh?" in
quired the other youth, evidently anxious
for a scrap.
"Oh, get next," angrily retorted the
pugnacious suitor.
"I'll do the punching and you keep
Nell from getting mixed up in the finish.
See! She's a dead game sport and if she
gets next that I'm going to do the guy
she will interfere. l want ter do him
good so that she will be ashamed to speak
to him. Are you wise?"
Of course his friend was wise, and
after awaiting a favorable opportunity,
just when the much admired ".sell" was
engaged in devouring a ham-and-egg
sandwich, the love-stricken youth walked
up to his rival and struck him in the
jaw. knocking him out. While waiting
for him to regain his feet "Nell," who
had witnessed the knockout of her new
admirer from a distance, rushed to the
scene and struck "Teddy" in the face
with the remnants of her ham-and-egg
sandwich. .... .. â
With his face besmeared with the fried
egg the belligerent youth turned to his
fair assailant and pleaded with her to
forgive him. . . I Â'
"It's all your fault," he tearfully re
marked, "yer know I'm stuck on yer, and
ver give me the worst of it. This dude is
not fit for you, as he can't fight enough
to keep hlsself warm."
After casting a scornful glance at the
prostrate dude, "Nell," evidently touched
by the remarks of her old suitor, threw
her arms around his neck and in burning
words declared her love for him. A few
minutes later the reunited couple were
lost in the mazes of a waltz, while the
bruised dude was busily engaged in
searching for a policeman to have his
assailant arrested.
On the return trip to the city there were
several "scraps," which were quickly
stopped -by the police.
A red-headed urchin almost created a
riot by attempting to sing "How I Love to
See My Poor Old Mother Work." He was
promptly seized by several young men,
who started to drag him to the railing
of the steamer, intending to throw him
overboard. Two policemen, realizing the
boy's danger, rushed to his assistance,
and after considerable trouble succeeded
ln saving him from being thrown into the
water. â

SHELL MOUND SHOOTING.
Targets Were Well Patronized by-
Skillful Marksmen.
A large number of skillful marksmen
gathered Shell Mound yesterday and
kept up a constant fusillade at the â¢
targets. The day was not a good one
from a rifleman's point of view on ac
count of the shifting light and a strong
wind. Nevertheless the shooting con
tingent adjusted their rifles and them
selves to the somewhat unfavorable con
ditions that prevailed and made some
very good scores.

WHIM WON.
Canoe Beat the Fleet in Oakland
Navy Race Yesterday.
The fourth Oakland navy race of the
season of 1899 was held yesterday on San
Antonio Estuary over the regular tri
aneular course of about six miles on an
ebb tide, with a fresh westerly breeze. At
the same time and over the same course
the 'Oakland Canoe Club held a mixed
race for sloops and canoes. The start was
by a single gun, and was maoe at 12:51
m. While maneuvering about before
the start the canoe Codia capsized near
the float of the Alameda Boat Club.
. She was bailed out, and started round
the course Â»__;ut nineteen minutes later.
Eight boats crossed the line in a bunch,
and presented a pretty signt. Of these
three were sloops and five canoes.
On the completion of the first round the
canoe Whim was in the lead, followed by
the sloop Surprise and the canoe Vitesse.
At tne end of the second round the
canoe. Whim was still in the lead, with
the sloop Flash second and the sloop Sur
prise third, the canoe Vitesse having
dropped out. At the finish the canoe
Whim was still first, with the sloop Sur
prise second and the canoe Nereid third.
None of the rest finished. The results are
shown In the table:

ALMON M. CLAPP DEAD.
Passing of the Former United States
Government Printer.
WASHINGTON, 'April 9.â Almon M.
Clapp, at one time United States Govern
ment Printer, died at his residence here
at 4:30 o'clock this afternoon in his 88th
year. Mr. Clapp was born in Connecticut
in # 1811, and came from one of the oldest
families in New England, his ancestors
having- come to this country on the Mary
and John soon after the landing of the
Pilgrims. He established the Aurora (N.
V.) Standard and later the Buffalo Com
mercial advertiser, and still later the Buf
falo Express. He was a delegate to the
Pittsburg convention that j founded the
Republican party, and in 1869 was ,to
pointed Public Printer. On retiring from
that office in 1877 be bought an interest
in the National Republican of Washing
ton. During the last twelve or fifteen
are he has been living in retirement,
but retained his interest in politics. At
the time of his death be was president of
the Ant?-Civil Service League.

NO PROSECUTION
OF GEN. LUDLOW
Lanuza Refuses to Grant
Ortiz's Request.
THAT INCOMMUNICADO LAW
MILITARY GOVERNOR INTER
FERED FOR AMERICANS.
Cuban Generals Have Not Yet Noti
fied Gomez of His Re-Election as
Commander-in-Chief of the
Insurgent Army.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
HAVANA, April 9.â Secretary of Jus
tice Lanuza will probably riot comply
with the request of Judge Ortiz of the
Guadaloupe District Court, Havana
Province, to prosecute General Ludlow,
the military Governor, . or "interfering:
to prevent Dr. Jolliffe and Mr. Barton,
Americans, being kept incommunicado,
as provided by the Spanish law."
If the matter is brought to an issue
the question will be whether an Amer
ican official has a right to issue an or
der contravening a Spanish law unless
supported by decree of the Governor
General. Judge Ortiz insists that the
incommunicado law is in full force, like
all other Spanish laws which General
Brooke's proclamation of January 1
affirmed were to be unchanged. Mr.
Ludlow intervened in the case of Jol
liffe and Barton because the Spanish
officials refused to allow the attorneys
and friends of the prisoners to have ac
cess to them. It was this condition of
things, that prompted his written in
structions to Judge Ortiz not to apply
the incommunicado rule to Americans.
All railway communication from Ha
vana eastward is suspended because
the strike of 200 employes of the united
railways affects the provinces of Ma
tanzas and Santa Clara, as well as the
routes to Guanajay, province of Pinar
del Bio, and to Batanbano, where the
steamships make their communication
with Havana. No freight or passengers
could be moved to-day. The inconveni
ence resulting is very great. It is hoped,
however, that the strike will be settled
amicably.
To-day the Cuban generals who yes
terday elected General Gomez com
mander-in-chief were to notify him
officially of their action. They had not
done so up to 7 o'clock this evening, but
it is reported that he is to be waited
upon by a committee during the course
of the evening. There are so many
contradictory reports as to their inten
tions and doings that the fact of their
accomplishing little or nothing is be
ginning to discredit in advance what
ever action they may ultimately take,
which, at the best, will be merely con
ferring an honorary distinction, as Gen
eral Brooke is preparing to handle the
payment of the Cuban army quite in
dependently of General Gomez.
A soldier at Matanzas yesterday, one
of a squad cleaning the courtyard of
the castle, found a Spanish gold piece.
The members of the squad immediately
staked off claims in Western style and
dug up the whole yard, finding coins
dated all the way from 1730 to 1808 and
worth 306 American dollars.
Sport in. the Handball Arena.
Every available inch of sitting and
standing room in the galleries at Phil Ry
an's handball court at 858 Howard street
was occupied yesterday afternoon by
the admirers of handball sport. The lit
tle ball was set in motion early and
made to hop at a lively gait until the
electric. lights cast their shadows upon
the last game of the day. In all there
were twelve four-handed games disposed
of. with a few single ones on the side to
settle the disputed claims of some crack
players who had a score to make or lose.

322 Market street, _. F.â (.Send for Circular.*HUNT FOR MOORE
ENDS IN FAILURE
Nevada County Officials
Discouraged.
SEARCHING POSSES RETURN
WILL REST UNTIL A TANGIBLE
CLEW IS RECEIVED.
Believe the Fugitive Murderer Either
Has Got Out of the County
or Is Sheltered by a
Friend.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEVADA CITY, April 9.â The murder
of Policeman Kilroy is still unavenged
and his slayer, Ed Moore, yet at lib
erty. The hunt for Moore in this vicinity
is practically at an end and the au
thorities have come to the conclusion
that their only chance to capture him
is to rest â¢-ii their arms until some .re
liable clew to his whereabouts devel
ops. If Moore is still hiding within the
boundaries of the count; the authori
ties feel hat by this time he will have
decided to emerge from his hiding place
and make a break for the lowlands and
liberty.
The weather looks threatening to
night, and should it become stormy it
is believed Moore will be forced to make
his way to a point where he will be pro
tected from the fury of the elements.
Once on the march Moore will have lit
tle chance to elude the officers. Photo
graphs and descriptions of the man
have been posted conspicuously in
every town and hamlet in this and ad
joining counties, and if Moore has not
already passed out of the county his
chances for doing so now are small.
Sheriff Getchell arrived at Chico last
night and immediately joined the posse
which has been searching for a trace
of the man answering Moore's .crip
tion who was seen in that vicinity
shortly before noon yesterday. Up to
a late hour last night no word was re
ceived from Sheritt Getchell as to the
failure or success of the pursuit. The
prevailing impression here is that the
Chico scent will lead the posse on the
heels of a harmless "Weary Willie," of
which species there are many traveling
at this time of the year.
With the passing days wanes the
citizens' iiope c-f avenging the death of
Kilroy. Moore has evaded pursuit so
long that the excitement has begun to
subside and it is doubtful now it Moore
would' be violently dealt with in the
event of his ing brought in alive.
There is, however, one disadvantage
under wnich the murderer would De
placed in connection with the possibil
ity of being lynched. There is a grow
ing feeling here that if taken alive and
given a trial Moore would plead in
sanity, and it is Just possible one man
in a jury of twelve would consider the
plea. Moo-re has the reputation of be
ing a peculiar man, and if life depended
upon it liis attorneys might attempt
to blossom his peculiarities into a case
of insanity in order to save him from
paying the penality for his crime. To
prevent the success of such a plan the
citizens might take the law into their
own hands. â¢
There are only two theories now as
to Moore's whereabouts which are be
ing seriously considered by the citizens
and authorities and they narrow down
to deiinite propositions: The fugitive
has cither succeeded In getting out of
the county or he has a friend some
where in the mountains close to the
scene of his rime who has supplied
him with food and shelter since the
night of his flight. When Moore' first
took to the woods general speculation
was overwhelmingly in favor of the
theory advanced by the searching
posses and others who knew Moore's
habits that Moore was hiding or being
sheltered by a friend somewhere in thi:,
vicinity. Now there la a growing senti
ment that the fugitive has succeeded
In placing many miles between himself
and the citizens of this county. Still,
scarcely anybody doubts that Moore
easily could have remained all this time
in the seclusion of the neighboring
woods and mountains if he had a faith
ful friend to supply him with provis
ions. The possibility of his evading
justice and remaining in this vicinity
without a friend is now considered quite
remote. Nine â  lays have passed since
Mo-ore murdered Policeman Kilroy, and
it is hardly possible that he could re
main in hiding in this vicinity so long
without having received aid of some
isort From a friend.
It is now generally believed that the
$1000 reward offered for Moore, dead or
alive will be the only successful fac
tor in bringing about his arrest or
death.
NEWSPAPER MEN TO
MEET AT BALTIMORE
Ninth Annual Conference of the In
ternational League of Press
Cm 1 ...
BALTIMORE. April 9.â The ninth
annul conference of the International
League of Press Clubs; which Will be
held from Tuesday to Friday of the
coming week, promises to be one of the
most successful in the league's history.
The Journalists' Club of this city will
be the host of the gates, who will
number about 200, representing press
clubs in all parts of the United States
and Canada.
The first business meeting will be
held on Tuesday afternoon. On
Wednesday morning the delegates and
guests will take the steamer Virginia
for Old Point Comfort, the former hold
ing a business session en route. The
last business session will be held in the
pavilion of the Hotel Chamberlain, at
Old Point, on Tuesday. Returning,
the steamer will reach Baltimore at 7
a. m. Friday.
. Anion* the questions to be discussed
at the business session will be the
establishment of a home for Indigent
and invalid newspaper men. Several
>Ites have been offered, including one
in Colorado, in connection with the
Childs Home for Inters. Mrs. E A
Robinson and Mrs. Isabella Tyson of
the Society of Friends' committee on
the purification of thi press, will be
received at the- first business meeting
and Mrs. Robinson will make an ad-*
dress urging purity of the press.
'l he- Baltimore delegates will urge
delegates from other States and Can
ada to secure the passage of a law
making communications to newspaper
men privileged the came as those to
priests and lawyers. Maryland Is the
only State which has this law.
The banquet to be tendered the dele
gates on Friday night in Masonic
Temple promises to be one of the most
elaborate dinners ever given in Balti
more. Some of the nation's most elo
quent alters will aid in providing an
oratorical feast. President McKinlev
has promised to be present if his health
and public business will permit, and he
is expected by the -mlttee to send a
favorable answer to the invitation early
in the week. Am-- the other notables
from abroad who have accepted invi
tations are Secretary of State John
Hay, who will speak upon "Newspaper
Men and Newspaper Clubs in Eng
land": Postmaster General Smith, Col
onel A. J. McClure and Congressman
Lemuel E. Quigg. Senators Chauncev
M. Depew, Joseph R. Hawley. Con
gressman Amos J. Cummings and
John Ac" ison Porter, the President s
private secretary have signified their
private secretary, have signified their
intention to attend if possible.
The social functions of the conven
tion, besides the banquet and steam
boat trip, which include a dance and
side trips of interest to Newport News.
Hampton Roads and Fort Monroe, con
templates an old-fashioned "commers"
and electric park trolley rides, recep
tions, lunches, theatrical parties and a
dinner for the lady delegates and visit
ors at Hotel Rennert. .".: J *
On Saturday the legates and guests
will be taken to Washington by special
train over the Baltimore and Ohio. and.
after calling on the President, will be
shown over the capital city by Balti
more Journalists and their Washing
ton friends.
BRYAN CONTINUES HIS
DEBATE WITH BELMONT
Insists That True Democracy Is Em
bodied in the Chicago
Platform.
LINCOLN. Nebr., April 9.â Colonel W.
J. Bryan to-day mailed his answer to the
last letter received from Perry Belmont
some weeks ago. The correspondence thus
far has dealt largely with the rival Jef
ferson dinners to be held in New York,
but Mr. Bryan ignores that matter and
devotes his attention to the differences
existing between himself and Mr. Bel
mont on the question of Democratic party
policy. His letter in part follows:
You ask. first, that I point out wherein your
utterances. are "unpatriotic, undemocratic un-
American anil ln conflict with the Democratic
creed as set forth in Jefferson's first inaugural
address."
We have no accepted standard by which to
determine whether a given opinion is patriotic
or American, but we have means of determin
ing whether an opinion is democratic and in
accord with the teachings of Jefferson. 1 pre
sume you use the word "Democratic" in the
party sense, Otherwise that term would be as
difficult to define as "patriotic" or "Ameri
can."
The right to determine what Is democratic
in a party sense belongs to the Democratic
party. The Chicago convention was more pure
ly representative than any other convention
held ln recent years, because the rank and file
of the party spoke on public questions through
Instructed delegates. The Chicago platform is
the latest authoritative definition of democracy
as applieM to existing conditions. If a minority
of the delegates to a national convention repre
senting a minority of the members of the
Democratic party has alright to determine what
is democratic, then each member of the party
has a right to define democracy for himself and
to assert that he is a better Democrat than
any one else.
Let me apply this principle to three question*
upon which you have taken a position in the
volumes sent me:
Firstâ Standard money.
Secondâ Paper money.
Thirdâ tax.
Jefferson favored the double standard as
against the single gold standard, and during
his administration our nation had the free and
unlimited coinage of gold and silver at the
legal ratio of 15 to 1. The ratio was changed
to 16 to 1- during Jackson's administration. The
Chicago platform pronounced against the .i.l
standard and In favor of the return to the free
and unlimited coinage of gold and silver at the
ratio of 16 to J without waiting for the aid or
consent of any other nation. As a delegate to
the Chicago convention you voted for a minor-
ity report which declared against independent
free coinage on the ground that It would not
only Imperil our finances, "but would retard or
entirely prevent International bimetallism, to
which the effort of the Government should be
steadily directed." As a de-legate to the In
dianapolis convention, two months later, you
supported a platform which declared in favor
of the gold standard without any declaration
in favor of international bimetallism. You may
be able- to explain why the minority rejected
I at the Indianapolis convention the plank for
which it fought at Chicago.
McKinley In IS9I charged in a public speech
that Mr. Cleveland during his first administra
tion was dishonoring one of the- precious
metals â discrediting sliver and enhancing the
price of gold. He insist. -d that Mr. -Cleveland
was trying to make "money -scarce ami there
fore dear," and added, "he would have In
creased the value of money and diminished the
value of everything- money the master
and everything else the servant." If Mi-Kin- -
ley then understood the real purpose of the
gold standard, as I believe he did. who could
think Jefferson capable of advocating a policy
which instead of securing equal and exact
justice for all. makes "money the master and
everything else the servant."
Your pronhecy that " a law to prevent wage
earners and salary earners from demanding
and urine payment in gold dollars .aid
not be a winning- Issue." evince? a tender so
licitude for the laboring man. I mlcht sug
pes* that bimetallism alarms you mere- than
It does the wage earners. It was not the em
ployes who Were frightened at the specter of
free silver in ISM: neither did the laboring man
Fhare your desire to add to the privileges of
the banks.
On December 20, IW, a year after the elec
tion, the Federation of Labor adopted the fol
lowing resolution-
"Resolved. That we declare ourselves most

STERNWHEEL STEAMER
SINKS IN THE OHIO
Passengers and Crew of the John K.
Speed Rescued Without Loss
of Life.
LOUISVILLE, Ky., April 9.â The big
rnwheel steamer John K. Speed, owned
by the Cincinnati and New Orleans Packet
Company, became unmanageable this
afternoon, owing to a treacherous cur- j
rent and the high wind prevailing, and I
struck the dam. The vessel careened,
almost toppling over, but righted again
and soon settled down to rock bottom. ;
The water rushed into her hold through
a big jagged hole aft of her boilers. . A ;
16-Inch pine derrick pole supporting the ;
staging broke in two and the staging
went overboard with a crash. It was a
miracle that several of the crew were j
not killed or injured. As it was, not one i
cf the 109 aboard was hurt in the least.
Life savers responded with the greatest
alacrity with all their lifeboats and were
beside the vessel immediately. With two
crews ferrying from the boat to the
shore all the- passengers were soon
brought to terra lirroa and the members I
of the deck and cabin crew were car- ;
lied over. - ;
The Speed was on her way to New Or- â 
leans and carried 405 tons of miscellane- !
ous freight. It is thought that at least
three-fourths of the Speed's value will
be saved, while the actual loss on the'
cargo will not be over $1000. The Speed
was valued at about $25,000. She ties on
the- rocks with the water over her main j
deck amidships. j â .- '-

"Trusts and Mono do lies." Colonel C. B.
Jones on "The Platform of 1900." The

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, APRIL 10, 899;
TRADE WITH OUR
NEW POSSESSIONS
Increase in Exports and
Imports.
PHILIPPINES AS A MARKET
GROWTH OF SHIPMENTS FROM
THE UNITED STATES.
Considerable Gain Shown in the Traf
fic With Cuba, Though the Bal
ance Is Aj-iinst This
Country.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, April 9. â Acting
Secretary of "War Meiklejohn Rave out
for publication to-day some interesting
statistics regarding the trade of the
United States with i" - Is'ind of Cuba.
Porto Rico and the Philippines, pre
facing them by calling attention to the
fact that normal conditions of trade
have not yet been fully restored be
tween the islands named. The returns
(or February show an increase of im-
ports from the islands in this country
amounting to $374, and a correspond
ing increase of exports from the United
States to the islands of $693,269. as com
pared with the same month a year ago.
The trade of the United States with â 
the Philippines, it is said, begins to '
show the natural growth due to the di- |
rection of the attention of American !
exporters to the archipelago. While
the exports from our ports to the Phil- i
ippine Islands for February of last
year amounted to only $1907, for the ;
same months this year they are more
than ten times as much, being $19,525. j
For the eight months ended with Feb- |
ruary 2S, IS9S, they were only $69,235, j
while for the corresponding period of
the present fiscal year they show a sain
of nearly 70 per cent, reaching $117.- !
021. Our direct importations from
Manila for February were $277,033. a *
gain of nearly 50 per cent over the !
same month last year. Notwithstand
ing the decline of imports into the :
United States from the Philippines i
during the months of active warfare,
the imports for eight months preceding
March reached $2,641,175. as compared
with $2,102,889 for a Similar period the
â¢year before.
Our trade with Porto Rico likewise
shows an excess of imports over ex
ports to that island. For the eight
months of the fiscal year the Imports
from Porto Rico to the United States
gained $60,000 in value over the former
year, being $587. against $525.554. Of
this gain more than one-half was in the
month of February, when the imports
of 1899 reached $124,618. against $89,186
for IS9S.
The trade balance between this coun
try and Porto Rico is now in favor of
our exporters, as for the same month
the exports were $267,619, or $170,545 in
excess of those of 1898. For the eight
months of the fiscal year the exports
amounted to $1,326,596. a gain of $12.
--596, developed regardless of the several
months when the ports of the island
were closed to our wares and mer
chandise.
Trade with Cuba has been in the
past several times as large as our trade
with Porto Rico and the Philippines
combined, and now is already showing
wonderful gains over the older figures.
A year ago in February, before the
war, we imported from the Island of
Cuba goods valued at $2,059,729. while
for the same month of the current year
the. figures were $2,307,940. For the
period of July 1, IS9B, to February 2S.
1899, the total imports to United
States ports from Cuba were $9,178 778
against $6,413,028 for the like period a
year earlier. This increase of more
than two and three.-ouarter millions
mainly occurred during the last several
months, when the crops were being
shipped.
The exports to Cuba have also pros
pered, those of January and February
showing an increase of over a million
dollars in value. For February. 1898.
the merchandise exported from this
country to Cuba was valued at $1,166.
--744. while for the same four weeks of
1899 the figures were $1,671,846. The
aggregate worth of the exports from
the first of last July to the end of Feb
ruary is given at $1,142,949, an increase
of 33 per cent over the preceding year.
DR. RAWSON COMMITS
SUICIDE IN AN ASYLUM
Was Defendant in a Sensational Di
vorce Case Decided at
Seattle.
INDIANAPOLIS, April 9.â Dr. George
A. Rawson, who figured in a sensational
divorce case in Seattle some years ago,
committed suicide at the Insane hospital
here to-day by hanging. His wife's name
was Nancy Norton, .and sue is a relative
of an ex-Governor of Virginia and a
cousin of Prince Yturblde of Mexico, who
created something of a sensation in
Washington several years ago. Dr. and
Mrs. Rawson were members of the exclu
sive circles of Seattle, and when she
brought suit for a divorce, making sen
sational charges, it was a nine days' won
der. The divorce was granted, and Raw
son, who was in New York at the time
disappeared. In 1891 Mrs. Rawson ap
peared in Washington with her cousin.
Prince Yturblde, and was received by Sec
retary Gresham and given social atten
tion. .;, :-:.-
Rawson appeared In Indianapolis about
three weeks ago and was found wander
ing about the streets by the police. He
was a victim of cocaine and morphine,
which had driven him Insane. He was
admitted to the insane hospital on April
7. and this mornin^ was found dead in a
closet. He made a rope of two towels, one
end of which he tied to a gas pipe anil
the other to his neck. He was slowly
strangled to death. He has property in
Seattle, and his relatives are supposed to!
live there. â _Â â¢* â â ?.<
REVOLVERS, KNIVES
AND CLUBS IN USE
Three Men Seriously Injured in a j
Riot on a Chicago
Street.
CHICAGO. April 9.â A riot in which
three men were -seriously Injured and a '
dozen others bruised by flying stones and |
pieces of iron, occurred to-day between J
about thirty structural Iron -workers, i
Revolvers, knives and clubs were used. I
The seriously injured are: Andrew ]
Delchert, R. J. Ross and Gottlieb Federer.
The fight occurred at 18 and 20 Evans
ton avenue, where a big -warehouse is in
course of construction. All the men en
gaged are union . workmen. It was
claimed by members of the union that an
agreement -whereby outside iron workers
were to do no inside' work was being vio
lated, and for several days the work In
side the building has been done under
police protection, but no officers were
there to-day. . - .

ALGER RETURNING
' FROM PORTO RICO
Secretary Pleased With General Hen
ry's Administration on the *V : ;
Island. \
SAN JUAN DE PORTO RICO, April 9.â
Secretary Alger, with his party,' left this
morning by the transport Ingalls, pro
ceeding direct for New York, where she
should arrive on Friday. During his stay
here he received many petitions from mu
nicipalities asking for financial help and
relief from excessive taxation.
The administration of General Guy V.
Henry, the Governor General of the Isl
and, is understood to be entirely satisfac
tory to the Secretary of War. The Secre
tary and the Governor General had a long
conference, and an understanding was
reached between them as to the future.
Miners Decide to Strike.
ST. LOUIS, Mo., April 9.â Top men in
the mines in the Southern Illinois district
have decided to strike as the result of
their employers' refusal to grant a raise
of 25 cents a day. This will tie up at
least 3000 men.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
> The weather Is generally cloudy over the
Pacific Slope. Light rain has fallen in the
western portions of Washington and Oregon
during the day.
The pressure has risen along the coast and
fallen In the Interior.
The temperature has fallen in all districts ex
cept Utah and Arizona.
Rain will probably extend to-night and Mon
day as far south as Cape Mendocino and the
extreme northern portion of California.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight, April 10, 1899:
Northern Californiaâ Partly cloudy Monday,
with showers in the extreme north portion;
cooler; fresh west wind.
Southern Californiaâ Partly cloudy and cooler
Monday; fresh west wind.
Nevadaâ Monday, probably with
showers; cooler.
Utahâ Cloudy Monday, probably with show
ers; cooler. .; a :â _ â  v. .* W.v "â â â

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographlc
Office, located in the Merchants' Exchange, Is
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners without regard to nationality and
free if expense.
Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison anil reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of Interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building at the foot of Market street Is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon anil dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Naval
Observatory, Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, If any. Is published
in the morning papers the following day.
W. S. HUGHES,
Lieutenant, U. S. N., In charge.

NOTEâ the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is. the mean, of the lower lav waters.
