NOTHING NOW
HEARD OF THAT
SILVER GAVEL
Its Mission Ends When the City
Treasury Is Empty.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call, I
908 Broadway, April 9.
THE forced donation of one-quarter of [
their salaries by a small army of city
employes Is no more of a joke |
than it is an act of charity. Nor can ]
the victims be blamed for the references
they are making to the fact that while
the treasury' is empty the shores of Lake ;
Merritt are being filled. In a word, the j
employes of the city are working as hard
as ever, hut are paying out of their own,
pockets for public improvements which !
are of benefit to but a small portion of
the city. /
Months ago influences were at work
that caused a revival of public spirit in |
Oakland. At least that was the way in
which the spasm was represented. Men
who stated that they paid taxes on i
116,000,000 worth of property commenced
a campaign to have certain improve- :
ments carried out. These improvements
were necessary, but Mayor Snow, then
Auditor, and several practical men de- j
clared that it would be far more Teasorf- i
able to spread the expenses of these im- j
provements over two fiscal years rather !
than to pauperize the city by taking all j
the money from one year's taxes. The '
claim that all the money could be spent
and the contract fulfilled before the end !
of this year Is now absolutely refuted 1 , '
for even a miracle of the ordinary kind
could not result in carrying out the vast
Improvements within the stated time.
All this was set forth at the time, but it
met with nothing more responsive than a
deaf ear.
The "Story of the Silver-mounted
Gavel" would be the truthful title of the
history of Oakland's misgovernment for
the past nine months. Night after night
those gentlemen interested in improving
the lake gathered at the council chamber
and by their presence aided in the pas
sage of legislation favoring the scheme.
On one occasion Auditor Snow remarked
that for sixteen millions to sit behind City
Councilmen was nothing short of mild
coercion, and he saiu if he had his way
he would say: "Gentlemen, go home and
leave the city's business to the city's
officials." However, piece by piece the
vast scheme went through, and when the
measure was passed appropriating all the
money from the fund of one year there
was tumultuous applause from the in
fluential lobby and a week later Presi
dent Heitmannâ one of the late "solid
six"â was presented with a silver-mount
ed gavel in token of the appreciation in
which he was held for the manner in
which he- hail fostered the impolitic legis
lation regarding Lake Merritt.
Last Monday night an ordinance was
Introduced stating that the city was
bankrupt, that the only money in sight
is $57,000, which cannot be touched even
to pay the salaries of employes. This
money is held sacredly for the benefit of
Lake Merritt, and not one cent of it can
be transferred. Further on the ordinance
contained figures reducing the salary of
everybody whose emolument is not fixed
by the charter, one quarter. Although it is
not pretended that these employes were
getting more than they deserved, there
is no choice under the circumstances.
Their salaries must be reduced or the
city will be unable to run. Mayor Snow
says that somebody must get hurt strug
gling out of the pit, and he set the ex
ample of cutting his own salary one
quarter with the rest. But when the ord
inance was read no silver gavel resounded
on the- president's desk, neither was there
an influential lobby to applaud. On the
contrary there were scores of unhappy
faces of men whose salaries ranged from
$25 tee (130 per month listening to the
words that required of them so large a
Bhare of their bread-and- butter fund. It
has been calculated that this retrench
ment of salaries will save the city about
$7000 by the end of this fiscal year, and
one of the deputies has also made the cal
culation that an assessment of one-twen
tieth of one per cent on $16,000,000 will
raise $SOOO.
The- first donation toward the deficit
of any size, it should be noted, came from
"a hated corporation" and amounted
to $5750.
Perhaps one of the most amusing fea
tures of the stringency in the city's purse
is the situation of Health Officer J. P.
Dunn. Dr. Dunn might almost be said to
have cut his own throat. Six months ago
an influential party drove around Lake
Merritt. Health Officer Dunn and several
members of the Board of Health were in
the company. The borders of the lake
were Inspected and every patch of green
slime or anything that suggested mi
crobes was shown to the doctors. Little
bottles were filled, specimens were cor
raled and for several days the bacteriol
ogists, the microscopists and the experi
mentalists of the Board of Health were
all busy making pictures and descrip
tions of gaunt death-dealing things which
Inhabited the lake. After a while a re
port was duly issued stating that the lake
was a menace to the public health; that
it was likely during the hot summer to
produce bacilli, microbes, colonies of
germs, anthrax, bubonic plague and a
few other things of which the' doctors
found mention in the text books. This
was all that was necessary. The Council
fell in line, marched along with the pro
cession and the salary of the Health Offi
cer has been cut one-fourth, said fourth
being his contribution toward the elimina
tion of these frightful creatures of the
lake.
There is some merit in Mr. Cuvelller's
attempt to repeal the ordinance forbid
ding the sale or smoking of cigarettes.
His main argument is undeniable. He
says that the ordinance- has not prohib
ited their sale, nor has it tended in any
way to improve public morals. It has
only operated to send the legitimate trade
of Oakland to San Francisco. The ordi
nance, it Is suggested, was passed in the
Interest of a firm of manufacturers who
sell cigarette tobacco but not ready-made
cigarettes, and ii was reserved for Mr.
Cuvellier to discover the ghost in the
woodpile. A State law makes It a crime
to sell to minors, and it is very doubtful
If a local law making it a crime to sell
to adults would stand. It has never been
tested simply because it has never been
enforced.
In the general desire of the new city
administration to stamp out gambling
there is danger that the crusade may be
carried too far. A measure of this kind
must be very clear and also beyond the
possibility of being ridiculed. At this
Urns can be found Is turned into a gam
session of Oakland. This cannot be de
nied. Every place wnere sufficient vic
tims can be found is turned into gam
bling den, and this Inclination to "amble !
Is rapidly increasing. In suppressing it
there must be a line drawn, and it is
useless to be blind to this fact. An or
-inance that will, in effect, make it a mis
demeanor to match nickels on a street car
to pay the fare, or to shake dice for a
drink, will not Tie popular, but will be
.subject to ridicule-. These are not the
?vlls which it is sought to reach, for per
nicious as they may be. these practices
nave never yet landed a man in jail. The
suppression of keno dens and similar in
itltutions is imperatively demanded. One
visit to these places will convince any-
Dody of their demoralizing effect. If any
thing could be more thoroughly depraved
:han a keno den, where 10 per cent of the
pool is held out, but returned to the win
ner in beer checks. I cannot conceive of I
It. In one joint there were 176 players, |
mostly youths, a few of them boys, sitting i
iround with cards In their hands, waiting |
for some one to win. breathing the foul- I
est of air. thick with vile smoke, and
worse, and noisy with oaths and rude
nessâand all this is not at "resent in de
fiance of the law. Surely no other argu
ment for the immediate passage of a j>rac
| tical measure is necessary. To this par
ticular den City Attorney Dow made a
| short visit of inspection a few nights ago,
i and although he has been a Police Com
. missioner for two years, he said he had
I no conception that such an abominable
; sight could be witnessed in Oakland. Be
fore he went home the special officer who
accompanied him had convinced him that
â¢ there were a'score of similar places, all
; doing a rushing business.
This legislation is admittedly of the
' most delicate kind. Oakland is near San
; Francisco. In the big city across the bay
i there is practically no restraint on such
: games, and the Oaklander seeking vicious
forms of diversion will spend his money
i across the bay. It is necessary not to do
anything which will cause people to spend
! money in the legitimate channels out of
: this city, but there is a tendency for
them to do so if sound, practical sense be
I not used in connect -~~ with an honest
desire for reform. i o promote local prog
; ress and to drive out the vicious element
â¢ of society, with a race track on one side
and a metropolis on the other, is a task
with which Oaklands's new p-ov rnment
i has now to deal, and it is not * light
I one.
The millionaire who has been sued for
a nittance for which he contracted, be-

It is probably safe to say that the ca_

The record of this city is being main
tained. The latest fad is to thank juries
who save you from prison or hanging.
Attorney Sawyer, after arguing for a

BERKELEY LIKELY
TO ABOLISH SALOONS
THE LEADING ISSUE OF THE
CAMPAIGN.
Local Politics of the University Town
Will Be Fought Out at the
Polls To-Morow.
BERKELEY, April 9.â The campaign
of, local politics In the university town
has closed, and to-morrow the polls will
show what attitude the residents of the
city have taken toward the questions
which have sprung up during its course.
The contest will not be a mild one. On
the contrary, opinion is very sharply di
vided over both principles and persons,
and the several factions are preparing to
wage war to the bitter end.
Chief among the issues Involved is the
one raised by the churches and the Wo
man's Christian Temperance Union on
the licensing of saloons.. It is more than
likely that the city will startle itself by
finding a heavy majority vote polled
against saloons. Such are the indications
at present, and such is the opinion of
several of the older politicians who know
the field thoroughly.
It will be startling because throughout
the campaign the issue has been kept un
der the surface apparently. The advo
cates of temperance have worked in si
lence, but they have worked very effect
ively. A week ago their force was hard
ly felt; the last few days have seen the
question they are agitating assume re
markable proportions. To-morrow It
will be the dominant issue at the polls,
and the opponents of the saloon-keepers
expect to bring all their influence to bear
in one supreme effort to carry their point.
The Woman's Christian Temperance
Union has this advantage as far as any
prejudice in its favor is concerned, of
having divorced the fight altogether
from personal or party politics. It has
allied itself with no candidate and with
no platform. Together with the local
churches and the State Anti-Saloon
League the effort has been to put the
question on the ground of pure principle
by an appeal to the moral sense of the
communtiy. The appeal, in the shape of
public addresses, campaign literature and
personal canvass, has been a strong one,
and its advocates are already predicting
success.
Among other points involved in to-mor
row's election the matter of abolishing
the office of City Superintendent of
Schools has fallen to the ground. The
complete expose made by The Call of
the attempt to cripple the School De
partment by forcing it to shift along
without system and organization arouse
so much Indignation on all sides against
those concerned in the scheme that they
were forced to change front and drop the
issue entirely. The candidates nominated
on the understanding that they would
do away with the office of City School
Superintendent still remain on the ticket,
but one and all have placed themselves
on record as retracting their former
stand.

BERKEYEY, April 9.â The State Sun

HE HAS BEEN ON DUTY
FOR THIRTY-FIVE YEARS
EVERYBODY who has had occa
|-^ sion to have a letter registered
I \ knows the venerable and polite
â ^"^ gentleman at the receiving win
dow of the main postoffice on Wash
ington street. It is believed that he
lives at the desk to which he has been
chained for so many, many long and
weary years. Nobody has ever seen
him on the street or anywhere else ex
cept at the desk where" he has spent
the better part of his life in writing
receipts on tissue paper and stamping
numbers and mysterious symbols on
the backs of envelopes given him by
the public. , â 'â â¢â â ; '.;'?â [ '. . .-.: .-..v..' ]-':
Yet he is human, has a home,, and
sleeps in a bed at night. His name is
M. G. Sawyer, and he is in his sixty
ninth year. For the past thirty-five
years he has sat by the window in the
old Postoffice and signed all kinds of
receiptsâ tissue paper and otherwise.
When he goes to heaven it is expected
that St. Peter will give him the same
old job in the registry department of
good souls. . 'â â '.]/.â -.
Mr. Sawyer first chained himself to
the registered letter desk on April 1,
â during the civil war, and a year
before President .Lincoln was assassinated. It was when Richard F. Perkins
was Postmaster. Perkins passed out of office and Holland Smith came in.
After him were James Coey, Napoleon B. Stone, Samuel W. Backus, W. J.
Bryan, Frank McCoppln, and the present Postmater, W. W. Montague.
Through all these changes of party administration old man Sawyer has stuck
to his post, a faithful servant of the Government, and is expected to remain

W. W. TAYLOR HEARD FROM.
His Family's Anxiety as to the Safe-
ty of the Popular Insurance
Agent Over.
BERKELEY. April 9.â W. W. Taylor of
Warring street, who was believed by his
family to have met with some untimely
end while traveling in the South, has
been heard from at last, and all anxiety
and apprehension as to his safety are
over.
Mr. Taylor is an agent of the Fireman's
Fund Insurance Company. He left Berke
ley about a month ago to visit the south
ern part of the State in the interests of
his firm. His family received letters from
him regularly for two weeks, but after
that all word from him suddenly stopped.
As he was very regular in his communi
cations his failure to write excited the
greatest alarm.
The last letter was dated from Los An
geles, and according to the police of
that city his friends and relatives here
sent telegram after telegram inquiring for
the missing man. The Los Angeles police
worked on two different clews, organiz
ing search parties in each case. Neither
attempt was of avail. One resulted
in the capture of an escaped murderer
who had stolen his liberty and was wan
dering in the hills at large. The other
brought to light an insane man who had
escaped from his ward. But no trace
could be found of the missing insurance
agent.
The family had already resigned itself
to hear a fatal report at any moment,
when a telegram from the City of Mexico
announced Mr. Taylor's arrival there. For
some reason or other his letters had not
been forwarded, and he had been obliged
to go farther south than he had expected
before leaving. He will reach Berkeley
from Mexico to-morrow on the southern
overland. â¢â¢.:.'

NOTICE Is hereby given by order of the Board
of Directors of the OCEANIC STEAMSHIP
COMPANY that a meeting of the stockholders
of said Company has been called by said
Board, to be held on FRIDAY, the second day
of June. A. D. 1899, at eleven o'clock ln the
i forenoon of said day, at the principal place of
business of said Company, at the building
where the said Board of Directors usually
I meets, namely, at the office of said Company,
| number 327 Market st., in the City and County
of San Francisco, State of California; that
| the object of said meeting Is to consider and
act upon the proposition that said Company
create a bonded Indebtedness of two million
five hundred thousand dollars ($2,500,000 00). in
United States gold coin, for the purpose of
raising money to complete the construction
of Its steamships and their equipment, for
use In the business of this corporation, and
to purchase and pay for any other property
within the purposes of this Company; and to
secure the bonded indebtedness so proposed
to be created by a mortgage upon its steam
and sailing ships and all other property of
said Company now owned or hereafter to be
acquired by said Company.
By order of the Board of Directors of the
Oceanic Steamship Company.
rCorporate Seal.] E. H. SHELDON.
Secretary of the Oceanic Steamship Com-
pany.

PROPOSALS.
PROPOSALS for furnishing all the furniture.
fixtures, finishes, etc., for the Hall of Justice
ana Morgue buildings.
Office of the Clerk of the Board of super-
visors of the City and County of San Fran-
cisco, April 4, 1899.
In accordance with resolution" No. 2587
(Fourth Series) of the Board of Supervisors,
sealed proposals will be received ln open ses-
sion of the Board of Supervisors on MON-
DAY AFTERNOON, April 17, 1899. from 2:30
to 3 o'clock, for furnishing all the furniture,
fixtures, finish's, etc., for the Hall of Justice
and Morgue buildings, In strict accordance
with the plans and specifications prepared by
Messrs. Shea & Shea, architects, and filed in
the office of the clerk of the Board of Super-
visors on April 3, 1899, copies of which can
be seen by intending bidders at office of
Messrs. Shea & Shea, architects, at No. ii
Montgomery street.
Bidders will bid on this work with the un-
derstanding that all the material mentioned
in the specifications shall be manufactured in
the State of California, and that all labor
necessary in the manufacture of said mate-
rial and all labor necessary In the erection
and completion of the work mentioned in the
specifications shall be performed by parties
residing in the State of California.
And it is to be distinctly understood that
eight hours shall constitute a day's work for
persons employed on the above work, and
that the minimum rate of wages to be paid
shall be two ($2) dollars "per day. .
Bidders will also be required to submit with
their bids a certified check on some bank In
the city and county of San Francisco in a
sum equal to one-tenth (1-10) of the amount
of their bid. and the party or parties to
whom the contract or contracts may be
awarded will be required to enter Into a bond
In a sum equal to one-fourth 04) 0 f the
amount of their contract, signed by two or
more jrood and sufficient sureties, to be ap-
proved by the Board of Supervisors, for the
faithful performance of the contract.
Bidders will estimate and state a price on

EDSON AVERSE
TO ACTING AS
A CATSPAW
Will Move to Reconsider the
Railroad Commission's
Action.
Blackstock Will Make a Stand in Favor
of Abandoning the Pending Liti
gation Without Delay.
Railroad Commissioners Edson, Lau
meister and Blackstock are not in the
Fame accord with one another as they
were apparently at the last session of the
commission, and there promises tee he- a
lively session to-day. . At the last meet-
Ing there was a unanimous vote on the
resolution rescinding the resolutions of
the former board," which provided for a
2r> per. cent reduction in freight rates in
the future and for an Immediate reduc
tion of 8 per cent in grain rates on the
]iÂ»ies of the Southern Pacific Qompany.
To "this resolution of rescission was at
tached a proviso suggested by Commis
sioner imeister that grain rates should
be reduced 8 per cent.
Blackstock was the father of the
original resolution. His idea was that the
passage of it allowed the suit of the
Southern Pacific to fall to the ground, as
it withdrew the- cause of the action.
Laumeister claims that the proviso
must be carried "in before. the resolution
of scission becomes effective and that
the Southern Pacific must dismiss its
case before any reductions can be made
by the commission.
Edson is opposed to the entire proceed
ings of the last meeting. He is not In
favor of the commission taking the initia
tive In any contemplated compromise, but
believes thai the first step should be
made by the railroad company and thai
this step should be the dismissal of the
pending suit. At the meeting to-day he
will move for a reconsideration of the
rescission resolution.
Commissioner Edson arrived from Ga
zelle and Commissioner Blackstock from
Ventura yesterday, to attend the meeting
of the board to-day. Both put up at the
Occidental Hotel, where they were inter
viewed-
"I intend to move for a reconsideration
of the resolution of rescission," said Com
missioner Edson. "I was never in favor
of It, and voted for it simply to be able
to move for a reconsideration, as I
thought we should have a better under
standing of the matter beiore taking
final action. I have some doubts of the
power of this board to rescind the action
of its predecessor. In this connection I
have submitted a number of questions to
Attorney General r-ord. One of these was
whether we had authority to rescind the
resolutions of reduction adopted by the
old board. Another was whether we had
the power to rescind the 25 per cent reso
lution without rescinding the v per cent
reduction on grain rates. I also suggested
that he secure the assistance of W. VV.
Foote and Robert Y. Hayne in the consid
eration of this great question, if he
thought it advisable, as they handled the
case in conjunction with the former At
torney General, W. F. Fitzgerald.
"I don't think we ought to compromise
with the railroad company until they
make the first move. They ought to dis
HIRSCH MEMORIAM.
Rabbi Levy Delivers an Address on
the Great Woman Philan
thropist.
Rabbi Levy held services at the Beth
Israel Synagogue last night In memoriam
of the Baroness Hirsch, the great Russian
philanthropist. The altar was decorated
in white, and the services were of great
solemnity.
The quartet rendered four selections:
"Man, His Days Are Like a Shadow,"
'(Â» Lord, What is Man?" "Rest. Spirit,
Rest," "Man, Art Thou Not Created From
Dust?"
"Many daughters have done well, but
thou excellest them all," was the subject
of Rabbi Levy's address. He said: "The
Baroness Hirsch is the greatest woman
of this century. She has given to charity
$25,000,000 without distinction of.creed. Her
goodnes has been showered unon all peo
ple-, upon all countries. To the poor refu
gees In New York and the working girls
of that city she has distributed over two
million of dollars. We in this city have
felt the gen.ii and ennobling effect of her
charity. She sent a check for $5000 to the
Mount Zion Hospital fund. To enumerate
nil her charitable deeds would 1111 vol
umes."
Rev. Dr. Locke on "Shams."
The subject of Rev. Dr. Locke of the
Central Methodist Church. Mission street,
was "Shams." The scriptural text for
the evening was St. Luke xii:l. On this
Dr. Locke said* "Hypocrisy is a grace
less excrescence of civilization. As truth
is at enmity to a lie, as purity shrinks
from corruption, so heaven abhors
hypocrisy.
"There are business shams. Much that
passes for shrewd financiering is noth

Commissioner Blackstock stood firmly
by his resolution and his interpretation
of it. "I understand that the resolution
of the former board was absolutely re

"The- proviso of Mr. Laumeister was sim

pending litigation will not saddle the

The 8 per cent reduction should not be

It is most needed, even if the reductions

cent. ;'ââ¢â¢_
"I have no fear of the commission be

on grain rates, while the temporary in
junction of the Circuit Court is still in
force. We will not be in contempt until
our reduced schedule of rates is served
on the railroad company, and that will
not be for some weeks. 1 don't think we
will have any trouble with the railroad
company in putting in the proposed re
ductions. We have nee understanding
with the company, but I base my views
em the fact that a compromise on the
basis we propose was made to Mr. La
Rue by Mills. I have also learned from
Mr. La Rue that he was strongly, in" favor
of such a settlement of the controversy.
I believe in doing business right out, and
not in beating the devil about the stump."
HUNTINGTON IS ILL.
The Aged Railroad Magnate Confined
to His Home by a Cold.
C. P. Huntington, president of the
Southern Pacific Company, was confined
to his home by Illness all day yesterday.
He had been Buffering from a cold on
Saturday and was given a prescription by
Dr. M. Gardner, his regular physician.
Yesterday the cold took a turn for the
worse, and owing to the absence of Dr.
Gardner from the city another physician
was called in.
It was stated at the house of the aged
and ill millionaire last night that the cold
had affected his kidneys and that he had
to take to his bed. It is believed that
there Is no serious danger, except what
may be caused by the advanced age of
the patient.

Olympic Club Tryout.
The Olympic Club Wheelmen held a five
mile tryout on the road between Hay
wards and San Leandro yesterday after
noon. The men started two minutes apart
and finished with the following times:
H. S. Bailey. 12:55 1-5; T. H. White.
12:57 2-5; George Kroetz, 13:15; R. A. Hen
derson, 13:33; Ed McNess, 13:33 3-5; Ed
Adams, 14:00 4-5; H. V. Smith, 14:06 2-5.
T. F. Meherin and T. G, Spillane acted
as starters, and John Elliott and E. G.
Montealegre as finish timers. William
Maekie, Ed Goeller and D. MacLeod were
scorers. The road was In fair condition,
but there was a stiff breeze blowing.
Accident to Mrs. James Otis.
Mrs. James Otis, while walking down
the steep hill on Taylor street from Pine
to Bush, yesterday afternoon slipped and
fell to the sidewalk and sustained a com
pound fracture of the ankle. A carriage
was immediately summoned and Mrs.
Otis was taken to her apartments at the
Occidental Hotel. Drs. Brig-ham and
Soule are in attendance upon her, and
while they apprehend no serious results
their patient will be confined to her room
for some time. Mrs. Otis is the widow
of James Otis, who was Mayor of this
city some time in the seventies.
1 Daughters of Liberty.
Martha Washington Council, Daughters
1 of Liberty, celebrated its fourth an
: niversary recently by a snowball party
; in the Shisls building. There was, in ad
i dition to the dance, a well-arranged short
programme of entertaining numbers, and
the event was one of the most pleasant
1 in the history of the council.
THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, APRIL 10, 1899.
UNIVERSITY OF
CALIFORNIA'S
NEW PRESIDENT
Likely to Be Benjamin
Ide Wheeler.
RUMOR GIVES HIM THE POST
:A ? â¢
THE CORNELL PROFESSOR MAY
SUCCEED KELLOGG.
He Refuses to Deny That He Has
Been Offered the Place, But Says '
He May Have â¢ Something
to Say Later.
There Is good reason to believe that the
regents of the University of California
have practicaly decided- upon the man
who is to succeed President Kellogg- at the
head of the big State educational institu
tion at Berkeley. It is more th.-vn hinted
that the new president will be. Benjamin
Ide Wheeler, professor of Greek and com
parative philology at Cornell University.
Professor Wheeler's name has been
i more or less prominently mentioned in
I connection with tin- position for some time
i past, and since his recent visit to Cali
fornia, when he lectured at the university,
the feeling that he would be selected to
take Kellogg- place has been growing
Steadily both here and at Ithaca, N. Y.
j In fact, a dispatch received from the lat
ter city last night says it is persistently
rumored at Cornell that the professor has
already been invited to accept the presi
dency at Berkeley and will in all proba
bility do so, and this rumor is given more
credence in view of the fact that the pro
fessor will not deny the report, but con-
I tents himself with saying he can neither
I deny nor affirm it, suggesting, however,
I that he may have something to say on
the subject in the course of a couple of
weeks.
Should Professor Wheeler be installed
as president of the State University the
Regents will have cause to congratulate
Prof. Benjamin I. Wheeler.
themselves upon securing a scholar and
educator who stands in the first rank and
one who' will be a credit to the institu
tion and to the Slate.

LAST HONORS
GIVEN TO A
FALLEN HERO
Obsequies of Major
McConnville.
IMPRESSIVE AND SOLEMN
COLORED TROOPS. NOW IN
CHARGE AT PRESIDIO.
Twenty-Fourth Infantry Takes Pos
session of the Famous Grounds
and Will Hereafter Com
pose the Garrison.
All the splendor and solemnity of a
military x funeral was yesterday afternoon
MAJOR McCONVILLE.
accorded Major Edward McConnville,
Who fell while leading the Second Idaho
Battalion at Manila February 5. Troops
B and M of the Fourth Cavalry and a
mounted band composed part of the mil
itary escort to the ferry, and a uniformed
representation of the Knights of Pythias
was also in the line of march. At Pythian
Castle Impressive funeral services were
held. McConnville was a member of
Excelsior Lodge No. 27, K. of P.; colonel
of Uniform Rank No. 4, K. of P., and a
member of Lewiston Commandery No. 2,
Knights Templar: also a member of the
Rose Croix Chapter. All of these orders
he joined at Lewiston, Idaho.
The procession came down Market
street after the- ceremonies were conclud
ed, and the body was shipped to Idaho on
the evening train. Accompanying it
were Mrs. McConnville. Harry McConh
ville, a son, who was with the deceased
on the line- of battle, and Mr. Arrant, a
brother-in-law. I
The remains arrived in San Francisco
on the Scandla last Friday.
This morning the Twenty-fourth In
fantry (colored) will take possession of
the Presidio. The regiment has been
there long enough to get settled in its
new quarters: its commanding officer has
been assigned to the command of the
post, and its regimental staff has been
taking hold of the various departments
until everything is ready to relieve the
cavalry from the post duties to which it
has attended for so long.
Colonel Freeman r.f the Twenty-fourth
took command of the post on Saturday.
His adjutant. Lieutenant Leach, will a*
sume the duties of that position during
the coming week. This morning the guard
will be composed of men of the Twenty
fourth, and then the only other men on
duty will lee the mounted cavalry patrol,
that lias always been kept along the bor
ders of the reservation. â  ':â â â 
MUSHROOMS
MAY HAVE KILLED
MRS. McNALLY
Wife of an Attorney
Succumbs.
AN AUTOPSY WILL BE HELD
LINGERED A WEEK AFTER THE
MEAL WAS TAKEN.
Relatives Suspect That Poison From
a Previous Illness Became Active
in the Blood and Caused
.!,;. Death.
The wife of Frank H. McNally, an at
torney residing at 723 Gough street, died
yesterday under circumstances that indi
cate she was a victim of poison introduced
into her system through the agency of
mushrooms. The matter was reported to
Coroner Hill, under whose instructions
Dr. Zabala will hold an autopsy this
morning. Mrs. McNally was 34 years of
age and was the mother of two children.
She was formerly a Miss Schaefer of
Marysville. :â¢ 'L :
The Coroner was informed that the
mushrooms to which the death of Mrs.
McNally may have been traceable were
eaten a week ago yesterday at the Nevada
restaurant on Geary street. The husband
and wife took dinner there that evening,
and among other things Mrs. McNally
partook of the fungi. No evil effects were
felt from them during that night or the
next day, and it was not until Tuesday
evening last that Mrs. McNally com
plained of being ill, not sufficiently so,
however, to necessitate a call for a phy
sician. She suffered considerably Tues
day night and "Wednesday morning, but
no suspicion was entertained that poison
was undermining her life.
Mrs. McNally continued to grow worse
as the week wore on until Saturday night
her condition was alarming. 'It was
then thought that she had a return of
peritonitis, which nearly caused her death
a year before. On this belief application
was made to a druggist in the neighbor
hood, who gave a prescription intended to
relieve her pain. She took the medicine,
but it did not have the desired effect, and
it was decided finally to call in a physi
cian.
Dr. J. H. Barbat was summoned at mid
night Saturday and prescribed for the sick
woman. He made a second call yesterday
morning at 5 o'clock and found his patient
dead, after having suffered great agony.
He notified the Coroner of the circum
stances, but that official, at the request
ol the relatives, allowed the body to re
main at the home.
The lapse of time from the partaking
of the mushrooms till the first symptom of
illness and the.- gradual sinking from
then on until death a week later makes
the case appear a strange one at first
glance, yet it is state. that such cases
are not absolutely unknown. The rela
tives of Mrs. McNally do not believe
that her deatu was caused in that man
ner. They believe that if any toxic
agent was at work it was one that had
lain dormant in the blood for more than
a year, when Mrs. McNally suffered from
peritonitis. "While Coroner Hill seems
inclined to think the same way, he says
that it might have been possible for Mrs.
McNally to have eaten the mushrooms
last Sunday, as stated, and not suffer
from them other than she did.
Muscarin, a narcotic alkaloid, is the
active principle of poisonous fungi. The
symptoms of Its presence are usually felt
within a few hours after the mushrooms
are eaten, but may not show themselves
for a considerable time. When the poison
does manifest its presence, however, it
is with considerable violenceâ
vomiting and a violent colic. Great
mental excitement is noticeable in the
sufferer, almost invariably followed by
coma. It is generally believed by medical
men that until the poison gets into the
circulation no great harm can be done.
Once in the blood, however, death in
variably ensues. -
Whether or not Mrs. McNally died as
a result of muscarin poison, the state
ment that she ate the mushrooms con
taining it at the Nevada restaurant last
Sunday is denied by the proprietor, N. P.
Doupv. He says that mushrooms were not
on the bill of fare at any time last Sun
day. _
OLD VETERANS AT VALLEJO
Enjoyable Reception Tendered Sol
Cahen Saturday Night.
Last Saturday evening a delightful re
ception was tendered to Sol Cahen, de
partment commander of the G. A. R. at
Vallejo, by the ladles of Farragut Relief
Corps No. 30, Hartford Circle No. 23 and
the veterans of Farragut Post No. 4, G.
A. R. The reception w;as held In Samo
set Hall, which was profusely decorated
In national colors and garlanded with
fragrant flowers. A fine literary and mu
sical programme had been arranged, and
at 8 o'clock the hall was packed.
Commander J. A. Andres introduced
Cahen, who made an eloquent address,
touching upon the lofty patriotism in
spired by the Grand Army and the lessons
to be learned by its existence. He was
followed by Comrade Simpson, after
which Judge A. J. Buckles of Solano
County paid a high tribute to the Vallejo
post. Dr. Plymlre made the next ad
dress. The reception closed with the ren
dition of the musical programme.
At the Veterans' Home in Yountvllle a
programme for the enjoyment of the old
soldiers there was rendered on the same
evening.
HE IS TIRED OF LIFE.
W. H. Roberts Tried the Morphine
Route, but Was Not Successful.
W. H. Roberts, a wood molder em
ployed by C. F. Shaw, 2135 Fillmore street,
swallowed a dose of morphine in a rear
room in the Globe saloon, 30 Turk street,
yesterday afternoon. He was taken to
the Receiving Hospital, where the usual
remedies were administered and Roberts
was pronounced out of danger. He had
left the following note addressed to the
bartender in the saloon:
Dear Fred: Good by. I have some clean
clothes down at J. Brown's room at the Royal
lodging house, on Fourth street, room 22.
Please see that I have them on. Speak a pood
word for me to Emma, at 110 Taylor street.
Let the boys know all. They are in Bakers
fleld. Send word to my little girl and wife,
room 37, 606 Montgomery street. Good by.
DAD.
I am sad at heart, but I can't do as I would
like to do. â¢ If I had a $20 piece It would save
my life this morning, but I have not got It,
bo good by. . DAD.
Roberts said he wanted a good long
sleep, as he was tired of life. He had
been divorced from his wife and had
nothing to live for.
San Mateo Line Improvements.
The San Francisco and San Mateo elec
tric line has completed its double track
as far as the Fairmount School, and the
service yesterday, with the new time
schedule, was excellent. Notwithstanding
the cold and disagreeable weather, there
was a large attendance at Glen Park, and
for about three hours in the afternoon
the car% were taxed to their utmost ca
pacity handling passengers. At Glen Park
the programme consisted of a concert by
the Tivoli Opera House orchestra and a
vaudeville bill of aerial gymnastics, com
edy sketches, musical numbers and tight
rope performances.

recommended by physicians for family and
medicinal use because It Is pure.
A Boy and a Rifle.
Julius Marks, aged 14, living at 449 Jes
sie street, was treated at the Receiving
Hospital for a gunshot wound in the
right foot yesterday afternoon. Marks
accompanied by some young friends'
went out hunting with a parlor rifle Near
Ingleside he accidentally discharged the
weapon and a bullet lodged in his right
foot. .
